# OT (sort-of) - Does it take math skills to do data processing?

### OT (sort-of) - Does it take math skills to do data processing?

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-08-22T23:04:43-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ak4c8s$1f0$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
I received the following humorous item in my email today.  For those of you
who haven't seen it before, I thought that it MIGHT explain some of the
things going on in data processing today.

    ***

Teaching Math;

Teaching Math in 1950:
A logger sells a truckload of lumber for 100. His cost of production is 4/5
of the price.
    What is his profit?

Teaching Math in 1960:
A logger sells a truckload of lumber for 100. His cost of production is 4/5
of the price, or $80.
    What is his profit?

Teaching Math in 1970:
A logger exchanges a set "L" of lumber for a Set "M" of money. The
cardinality of set "M" is 100. Each element is worth one dollar. Make 100
dots representing the elements of the set "M." The set "C", the cost of
production contains 20 fewer points than set "M." Represent the set "C" as a
subset of set "M" and answer the following question:
    What is the cardinality of the set "P" of profits?

Teaching Math in 1980:
A logger sells a truckload of lumber for 100. His cost of production is $80
and his profit is $20. Your assignment:
    Underline the number 20.

Teaching Math in 1990:
By cutting down beautiful forest trees, the Logger makes $20.
    What do you think of this way of making a living?
    Topic for class participation after answering the question:
    How did the forest birds and squirrels feel as the logger cut down the
trees?
        There are no wrong answers.

Teaching Math in 2002:
A logger sells a truckload of lumber for 100. His cost of production is
$120.
    How does Arthur Andersen determine that his profit margin is $60?
```

```
JerryMouse wrote:
> "Grinder" <grinder@no.spam.maam.com> wrote in message
> news:ak8str02n3i@enews4.newsguy.com...
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> Doesn't this depend on what the definition of "is" is?

Clever answer, but no. As I said above, no one's much interested in the 
facts of this matter, but I'll post some anyway:-)

Gore did not claim to have "invented the Internet", he *did* say, to 
Wolf Blitzer, that he "took the initiative in creating the Internet", 
which is a very different thing. See below how he actually "took the 
initiative in creating the Internet", i.e., recognized the 'Net's 
importance and worked in Congress to get it funded. Two people widely 
regarded as the fathers of the Internet, Robert Kahn & Vinton Cerf, 
published an article in 2000 to the effect that Gore's support and 
resulting funding were crucial to the development of the 'Net. Here's 
part of an article from:

http://199.97.97.16/contWriter/cnd7/2000/10/20/cndin/0737-0012-pat_nytimes.html

which explains Gore's role and quotes Vint Cerf:

   --------------------------------------------------------------

   Through the 1980s and early 1990s, as personal computers soared from 
a curiosity owned mostly by techie hobbyists and a few companies to a 
widespread commodity, anyone wanting to link a computer to a network had 
to choose from among the many private network systems available - 
Delphi, CompuServe, Prodigy, Genie, Bix, and a host of others. Each had 
its own e-mail system, its own bulletin boards to share information and 
search for answers on specific topics, and its own specialized services. 
None allowed any connection to the world outside the individual network 
and its subscribers.

       In addition, the Internet was still strictly limited to use on 
college campuses and in research labs. Private users did not have access 
to it, and no commercial uses were allowed.

       What changed?

       That's where Al Gore comes in. Technical histories of the 
Internet refer mainly to the technical milestones along the way. Among 
all the techno-whizzes that get the credit, only one legislator is 
mentioned - Gore - despite the fact that every stage of the Internet's 
evolution was funded and directed by federal grants and initiatives.

       Gore was widely credited in histories written long before the 
vice president's oft-derided comment to CNN reporter Wolf Blitzer that 
he ``took the initiative in creating the Internet.''

       Gore is credited by the technological cognoscenti for having 
sponsored legislation that helped launch the expansion of the fledgling 
Internet to ever-wider uses. As early as 1986, Gore articulated a vision 
of widespread connected computing. In 1989, he said that ``the creation 
of this nationwide network . . . will create an environment where work 
stations are common in homes and even small businesses.''

       Two years later, he introduced a followup bill to expand access 
to the network, saying, ``In the future, I think we will see computers 
and networks used to teach every subject from kindergarten through grade 
school.''

       None of these histories comes close to giving him credit for the 
``creation'' of the Internet. One account, written by Vinton Cerf 
(widely known, though he eschews the title, as ``the father of the 
Internet''), states: ``I think the vice president is very deserving of 
credit for his active support for the Internet and the businesses that 
depend on it daily.''

       Cerf, now a vice president at MCI-Worldcom, added that ``his 
remark was almost certainly a slip of the tongue, because he'd be quite 
correct to say `I helped create the Internet' - because of his work to 
provide an environment of support for research, technology transfer and 
e-commerce initiatives so fundamental to the Internet today.''

   -------------------------------------------------------------

 From another article published by Vint Cerf:

"Then-Senator Gore's 1986 legislation calling for the interconnection of 
the Centers using fiber optic technology ultimately led the 
administration to respond with the High Performance Computing and 
Communications (HPCC) Initiative. "

http://www.cs.washington.edu/homes/lazowska/cra/networks.html

   -------------------------------------------------------------

Finally, from:

http://www.interesting-people.org/archives/interesting-people/200009/msg00052.html

an email from Kahn & Cerf addressed to Dave (Farber) and Declan (McCullagh):


Subject: IP: Al Gore's support of the Internet, by V.Cerf and B.Kahn [ I 
second this djf]

     * From: Dave Farber <farber@cis.upenn.edu>
     * Date: Sat, 30 Sep 2000 08:29:48 -0400

---------- Forwarded message ----------
Date: Thu, 28 Sep 2000 17:43:58 -0400
From: vinton g. cerf <vcerf@MCI.NET>
To: Declan McCullaugh <declan@well.com>, farber@cis.upenn.edu
Cc: rkahn@cnri.reston.va.us
Subject: Al Gore and the Internet

Dave and Declan,

I am taking the liberty of sending to you both a brief
summary of Al Gore's Internet involvement, prepared by
Bob Kahn and me. As you know, there have been a seemingly
unending series of jokes chiding the vice president for
his assertion that he "took the initiative in creating
the Internet."

Bob and I believe that the vice president deserves significant
credit for his early recognition of the importance of what has
become the Internet.

I thought you might find this short summary of sufficient
interest to share it with Politech and the IP lists, respectively.

==============================================================

Al Gore and the Internet

By Robert Kahn and Vinton Cerf
Al Gore was the first political leader to recognize the importance of 
the Internet and to promote and support its development.

No one person or even small group of persons exclusively "invented" the
Internet. It is the result of many years of ongoing collaboration among
people in government and the university community.  But as the two 
people who designed the basic architecture and the core protocols that 
make the Internet work, we would like to acknowledge VP Gore's 
contributions as a Congressman, Senator and as Vice President.  No other 
elected official, to our knowledge, has made a greater contribution over 
a longer period of time.

Last year the Vice President made a straightforward statement on his
role.  He said: "During my service in the United States Congress I took 
the initiative in creating the Internet."  We don't think, as some 
people have argued, that Gore intended to claim he "invented" the 
Internet. Moreover, there is no question in our minds that while serving 
as Senator, Gore's initiatives had a significant and beneficial effect 
on the still-evolving Internet. The fact of the matter is that Gore was 
talking about and promoting the Internet long before most people were 
listening.  We feel it is timely to offer our perspective.

As far back as the 1970s Congressman Gore promoted the idea of high 
speed telecommunications as an engine for both economic growth and the
improvement of our educational system.   He was the first elected 
official to grasp the potential of computer communications to have a 
broader impact than just improving the conduct of science and 
scholarship. Though easily forgotten, now, at the time this was an 
unproven and controversial concept.  Our work on the Internet started in 
1973 and was based on even earlier work that took place in the mid-late 
1960s. But the Internet, as we know it today, was not deployed until 
1983. When the Internet was still in the early stages of its deployment, 
Congressman Gore provided intellectual leadership by helping create the 
vision of the potential benefits of high speed computing and 
communication.  As an example, he sponsored hearings on how advanced 
technologies might be put to use in areas like coordinating the response 
of government agencies to (sic) natural disasters and other crises.

As a Senator in the 1980s Gore urged government agencies to consolidate
what at the time were several dozen different and unconnected networks 
into an "Interagency Network."  Working in a bi-partisan manner with 
officials in Ronald Reagan and George Bush's administrations, Gore 
secured the passage of the High Performance Computing and Communications 
Act in 1991.  This "Gore Act" supported the National Research and 
Education Network (NREN) initiative that became one of the major 
vehicles for the spread of the Internet beyond the field of computer 
science.

As Vice President Gore promoted building the Internet both up and out, 
as well as releasing the Internet from the control of the government 
agencies that spawned it.  He served as the major administration 
proponent for continued investment in advanced computing and networking 
and private sector initiatives such as Net Day. He was and is a strong 
proponent of extending access to the network to schools and libraries. 
Today, approximately 95% of our nation's schools are on the Internet. 
Gore provided much-needed political support for the speedy privatization 
of the Internet when the time arrived for it to become a 
commercially-driven operation.

There are many factors that have contributed to the Internet's rapid 
growth since the later 1980s, not the least of which has been political 
support for its privatization and continued support for research in 
advanced networking technology.  No one in public life has been more 
intellectually engaged in helping to create the climate for a thriving 
Internet than the Vice President.  Gore has been a clear champion of 
this effort, both in the councils of government and with the public at 
large.

The Vice President deserves credit for his early recognition of the 
value of high speed computing and communication and for his long-term 
and consistent articulation of the potential value of the Internet to 
American citizens and industry and, indeed, to the rest of the world.


Version 1.2
Word count: 709


=================================================================
WorldCom
22001 Loudoun County Parkway
Building F2, Room 4115, ATTN: Vint Cerf
Ashburn, VA 20147
Telephone (703) 886-1690
FAX (703) 886-0047
```

```

"Liam Devlin" <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net> > >
> >
> > Gore: "...took the initiative in creating the internet..." vs. Reporter:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> which is a very different thing.

No it's not. "Took the initiative in creating the Internet" is Gore-speak
for "(I) invented the internet." Let's parse:

"Took the initiative" = start by taking the first step
"creating" = cause to come into being, construct from nullity

vs.

"Invent" = produce or contrive something previously unknown by ingenuity or
imagination

The distinctions I see are "imitative" implies others followed after the
first step was taken whereas "invent" implies the whole item came into being
without further modification: "initiative in creating" is the first step of
"invention," and "imitative" is a moment, an instant, whereas "invention"
implies a discrete time interval.

As far as determining whether Gore is a lying weasel, the difference between
"invent" in "initiate" is vanishingly small.
```

```
JerryMouse wrote:
> "Grinder" <grinder@no.spam.maam.com> wrote in message
> news:ak8str02n3i@enews4.newsguy.com...
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> Doesn't this depend on what the definition of "is" is?

To give you a little background on Declan McCullough, who wrote the 
Wired article:

http://groups.yahoo.com/group/cyberia-l/message/32352

From:  Seth Finkelstein <sethf@M...>
Date:  Tue Oct 17, 2000  5:37 pm
Subject:  Re: Declan's non-retraction retraction on Gore/Internet

ADVERTISEMENT

To begin, speaking of juvenile I'd like to note the little
shot which is in the meta-tag of the _Wired_ article:

<http://www.wired.com/news/politics/0,1283,39301,00.html>
<meta name="abstract" content="Humphrey Bogart never said, 'Play it
again, Sam,' and Al Gore never said, 'I invented the Internet.' But
both guys said similar things that took on lives of their own. By
Declan McCullagh.">

As has been pointed out _ad nauseum_ in these threads, Al Gore
made a perfectly reasonable comment about his work in Congress, which
was willfully misreported and attacked as a supposed technical boast.
Declan now spins:

"That statement was enough to convince me, with the encouragement of my
then-editor James Glave, to write a brief article that questioned the
vice president's claim."

Let's remember how Declan's article "questioned the ... claim":

<http://commons.somewhere.com/rre/2000/RRE.Al.Gore.and.the.Inte.html>
"The question of Gore's credit for the Internet is not submitted to
someone who was actually there, like Dave Farber or Joe Traub, but to
a guy from a Republican think tank." [Phil Agre]

Rather, Declan "questioned" via "Steve Allen, vice president for
communications at the conservative Progress and Freedom Foundation."
<http://www.wired.com/news/news/politics/story/18390.html>

Back to Declan's current damage control:

"Republicans on Capitol Hill noticed the Wired News writeup and
started faxing around tongue-in-cheek press releases -- inveterate
neatnik Trent Lott claimed to have invented the paper clip -- and
other journalists picked up the story too."

Now, a few times Declan has said e.g. (I still giggle at this)

"If there was no story in Al Gore's boast, other journalists would
have simply ignored it." <http://www.egroups.com/message/cyberia-l/31896>

There's a grain of truth in that Declan isn't quite so
dangerous all by himself. But he often teams-up with more powerful
people, thereby creating an effect more toxic than his own megaphone,
in effect turning that into an amplifier. In the case of the Al Gore
fabrications, Declan elides his role in that he was actively flacking
"House Majority Leader Armey"'s echo of his story:

<http://www.politechbot.com/p-00285.html>
"House Majority Leader Armey on Gore "inventing the Internet"

"I called up Armey's office when I got this. They told me they learned about
Gore's remarks from my Wired News story, which is at:
http://www.wired.com/news/news/politics/story/18390.html

Back to Declan-now:

"My article never used the word "invented," but it didn't take long for
Gore's claim to morph into something he never intended."

I wish the _Salon_ debunking article
(<http://www.salon.com/tech/col/rose/2000/10/05/gore_internet/index.html>)
hadn't missed this.

There are *two* articles by Declan McCullagh relevant here. TWO.
He did not use the word "invent" in the *first* article, but flogged it
in the *second* article. According to the debunking by Phil Agre:

<http://commons.somewhere.com/rre/2000/RRE.Al.Gore.and.the.Inte.html>
<begin quote>
"The original Wired article quotes Gore's famous words and glosses them
as claiming to be "father of the Internet" and "took credit for the
Internet". But the word "invented" does not appear in the text. ...
Although my search facilities are not the best, I cannot find any
use of the word "invented" in the media until it appears in another
Wired News article 12 days later:

http://www.wired.com/news/politics/0,1283,18655,00.html

This article reads in part as follows:

Al Gore's timing was as unfortunate as his boast. Just as
Republicans were beginning to eye the 2000 presidential race
in earnest, the vice president offered up a whopper of a tall
tale in which he claimed to have invented the Internet.

This is an important part of the echo-chamber effect. Start with a
fact, then circulate a paraphrase of that fact that makes it sound
slightly damning without actually falsifying it. Then once that
paraphrase becomes widely circulated, circulate a paraphrase of the
paraphrase that sounds even more damning. Repeat. In this case,
many paraphrases were circulated. ..."
<end quote>

Oh, but let's remember, Declan's story now is that he just
"questioned the ... claim".

I'll skip the rest of the current article, it's just some
miscellaneous Libertarian editorializing and rim-shots, for example:

"But it's also difficult to argue with a straight face that the
Internet we know today would not exist if Gore had decided to
practice the piano instead of politics."

Now for my editorial comments: I stand in awe at Declan's
utter shamelessness, but even more so at his ability to gain
reputation and self-promotion from *both* ends of his complete
fabrication. For *months*, he's been contemptuously dismissing people
who called him on his hatchet-job, such as just recently:

<http://www.egroups.com/message/cyberia-l/31896>
"Finally, I note, as I did in my column on Saturday
(http://www.wired.com/news/politics/0,1283,39172,00.html) that
Democratic partisans are rallying to defend Al. Not much time left
before the election, small surprise."

<http://www.egroups.com/message/cyberia-l/32024>
"Phill, my latest report was neither inaccurate nor misleading. There
seems to be a resuscitation effort going on. My only regret was that I
mixed metaphors in the first paragraph: I should have written "buff
his tarnished image." Sigh.

And I'm not about to change what I write or how I write based on the
efforts of Democratic partisans such as you (and I note that Phill
earlier today complained to my editors)."

<http://www.egroups.com/message/cyberia-l/32025>
"Andrew, I'm sorry to say it, but you're a fool or a knave, or both.

In my original article, that some seem to think caused this fuss
(and allegedly imperiled Gore's campaign!), I quoted Gore's statements,
and linked to the transcript:"

<http://www.egroups.com/message/cyberia-l/32027>
"But you're right on one point: This conversation is tiresome. When
you've got Democrats defending Gore on a partisan basis, logic has
little part of the discussion. There seems to be little reason for me
to continue here, so I'll bow out unless someone says something
worth responding to."


Now, admits Declan, "... the truth is that Gore never did
claim to have "invented" the Internet." (though Declan can't even say
that without a preliminary bashing, the sentence starts "The short
answer is that while even his supporters admit the vice president has
an unfortunate tendency to exaggerate, ...")

Amazing. Journalism means never having to say you're sorry.

I wish to declare that one of my deepest and most heartfelt
personal regrets is my having helped Declan, years ago, when he was
starting out. I fully expect him to do a vicious hatchet-job on me if
he ever gets a chance, and that's a significant factor for my avoiding
legal risk and not doing free-speech activism.

__
Seth Finkelstein Consulting Programmer sethf@s... http://sethf.com
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT (sort-of) - Does it take math skills to do data processing?

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu>
- **Date:** 2002-08-26T17:08:42+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<akdn8r$d6v$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<ak4c8s$1f0$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <Aly99.132217$EJ4.4122114@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net> <RDL99.165558$2p2.7398483@bin4.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com> <ak8nbs024qs@enews4.newsguy.com> <ak8str02n3i@enews4.newsguy.com> <qF1a9.50602$%v4.1456651@e3500-atl2.usenetserver.com> <3D692000.194ECAAA@shaw.ca> <anfa9.5422$2E6.2196968@newssvr28.news.prodigy.com> <akddqe$7ok$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <yQra9.4995$yt3.2105167@newssrv26.news.prodigy.com>`

```

On 26-Aug-2002, "Michael Mattias" <michael.mattias@gte.net> wrote:

> > I guess the maximum could go over 10 years - Could Clinton be elected Vice
> > President or Speaker of the House or even do a Gerald Ford?
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Vice-President of the United States."

But amendment XXII doesn't address eligibility to become president - only his
eligibility to be elected president.   The Supreme Court could address whether
that applies or not, but it isn't an open and shut case here.  It may rule one
way for someone to be elected vice president, and the other way for a Gerald
Ford.   It adds specifically that it does "not prevent any person who may be
holding the office of President, or acting as President"... from holding that
office.

Certainly if a two term president became Speaker of the House and a disaster
hit, advancing the holder of that post to President, this Amendment wouldn't
preclude him of becoming president again.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Presidential eligibility.

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2002-08-26T14:11:30-05:00

```

On 26-Aug-2002, "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> wrote:

> > Certainly if a two term president became Speaker of the House and a
> disaster
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> succession, because she was not born in the US.

The Constitution says "No person except a natural born Citizen, or a Citizen of
the United States, at the time of the Adoption of this Constitution, shall be
eligible to the Office of the President;".   (Boy the Constitution has funny
punctuation).   So Madeleine Albright cannot be president, elected or not.  
Amendment XXII limits when someone can be ELECTED president - but not whether
someone is qualified to BE president.   Being elected and being president are
two different things - as Gerald Ford has demonstrated.   So unless/until closed
by the Supreme Court - there is a loophole.
```

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote:
>
> The Constitution says "No person except a natural born Citizen, or a Citizen
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> unless/until closed by the Supreme Court - there is a loophole.

That's an interesting point.  As already pointed out, the Republicans
pushed that amendment through to stop FDR from running again, of
course.  If the amendment had said flatly that no one could hold the
office, rather than no can be elected, FDR would have been out, just
like that, dumped without impeachment.  I have no doubt that, during
wartime, this was scrutinized carefully (certainly by the Democrats,
if no one else ;-).  IIRC, FDR did continue to hold the office after this
amendment was enacted (i.i. it was passed before his death).  Then
it seems to follow logically that a person could theoretically become
President again, and hold the office longer than 10 years.  Since the
amendment only refers to being elected, it would seem an artificial
distinction no in the language, to have let FDR continue as President,
then prohibit anyone else from taking the office as we are discussing.
```

```

Liam Devlin <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net> wrote in message
news:OMxa9.8089$yl3.116773@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net...
> Judson McClendon wrote:
[snip]
> >
> > That's an interesting point.  As already pointed out, the Republicans
> > pushed that amendment through to stop FDR from running again, of
> > course.
[snip]

Actually the amendment was not proposed until almost
two years after FDR died. It took almost four more years
for ratification.

From the Collier's Encyclopedia article on
ROOSEVELT, FRANKLIN DELANO
"On Apr. 12, 1945, he complained of a severe headache, lost
consciousness, and died of a massive cerebral hemorrhage
that afternoon."

From Senate Document 103-6, The Constitution of the United
States of America: Analysis and Interpretation:

"The Twenty-second Amendment was proposed by Congress
on March 24, 1947, having passed the House on March 21,
1947, Cong. Rec. (80th Cong., 1st Sess.) 2392, and having
previously passed the Senate on March 12, 1947. Id., 1978.
It appears officially in 61 Stat. 959. Ratification was completed
on February 27, 1951, when the thirty-sixth State (Minnesota)
approved the amendment, there being then 48 States in the
Union. On March 1, 1951, Jess Larson, Administrator of
General Services, certified that it had been adopted by the
requisite number of States. 16 Fed. Reg. 2019."
```

```
Since the
> amendment only refers to being elected, it would seem an
artificial
> distinction no in the language, to have let FDR continue as
President,
> then prohibit anyone else from taking the office as we are
discussing.

This article appears related to this discussion:
http://www.cnn.com/2000/LAW/08/columns/fl.dorf.goreclinton.08.0
```

```

Howard Brazee <howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote in message
news:ake33b$jvs$1@peabody.colorado.edu...
>
> On 26-Aug-2002, "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> The Constitution says "No person except a natural born Citizen, or a
Citizen of
> the United States, at the time of the Adoption of this Constitution, shall
be
> eligible to the Office of the President;".   (Boy the Constitution has
funny
> punctuation).   So Madeleine Albright cannot be president, elected or not.
> Amendment XXII limits when someone can be ELECTED president - but not
whether
> someone is qualified to BE president.   Being elected and being president
are
> two different things - as Gerald Ford has demonstrated.   So unless/until
closed
> by the Supreme Court - there is a loophole.

You are talking about the most powerful office on earth.
No supposition that you can come up with can get around
the clear rules of succession.  There is NO loophole.

The Presidential Succession Act of 1947, signed by President Harry Truman,
established the current order of succession:

The Vice President Richard Cheney
Speaker of the House John Dennis Hastert
President pro tempore of the Senate Robert Byrd
Secretary of State Colin Powell
Secretary of the Treasury Paul O'Neill
Secretary of Defense Donald H. Rumsfeld
Attorney General John Ashcroft
Secretary of the Interior Gale A. Norton
Secretary of Agriculture Ann M. Veneman
Secretary of Commerce Donald Evans
Secretary of Labor Elaine Chao
Secretary of Health and Human Services Tommy G. Thompson
Secretary of Housing and Urban Development Melquiades Rafael Martinez
Secretary of Transportation Norman Yoshiro Mineta
Secretary of Energy Spencer Abraham
Secretary of Education Roderick Paige
Secretary of Veterans Affairs Anthony J. Principi

NOTE: An official cannot succeed to the Presidency
            unless that person meets the Constitutional requirements.

Your loophole doesn't exist.

So, your interpretation of the law is based on incomplete information.
The limit is ten years exactly, because if someone succeeds to the position
instead of being elected, and serves more than two years of the term
to which someone else was elected, then that term is treated as an elected
term
of office with respect to term limits, and that person may then only be
elected
once in his/her own right.

It would be nice is all COBOL programs were this tight with respect to
possibility of events.

Bottom line:
It will take a Constitutional Amendment to put Bill Clinton back at the
helm.  Another President Clinton, however, may only be 6 years in the
future.
```

```
"Hugh Candlin" <NoPersonalMailPlease@MyDot.Com> wrote:
>
> So, your interpretation of the law is based on incomplete information.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> elected once in his/her own right.

Actually, I think everyone agrees that a person could not be elected
President after serving more than 6 years, for the amendment is
very clear on this.  But the question is, "Can someone succeed to the
Presidency after being elected twice?"  Nothing you said here makes
that any clearer.  Going literally by the Constitution, I don't see why
not.  Unless the Constitution puts a limit on how long a President can
hold office, or specifically addresses succession, I don't see any real
basis for preventing it, unless several activist Supreme Court justices
get involved, in which case all bets are off.

Of course, this is all pretty theoretical, because it is very unlikely
anyone would accept a lesser office after being President.  Very few
politicians, if any, could show that kind of humility these days.  :-)
```

```
NOTE: An official cannot succeed to the Presidency
            unless that person meets the Constitutional requirements.

The officials referred to are Cabinet officials.

he personally would not be eligible under the Constitutional requirements,
even though the position he held is ranked in the line of succession.

Melquiades Rafael Martinez, who is named in the line of succession,
was born in Cuba, and is therefore ineligible.

you should not attempt to maintain a high profile in world affairs,
although Jimmy Carter seems to be testing that theory lately.

The presidential election of 2008 could be very interesting
if the voters are presented with the unprecedented choice
between a woman president (Clinton) and a black president (Powell).

So far, a white has always prevailed in the presidential poll over any other
race,
and a male chosen instead of a female.  That precedent goes out the window
if the voters are forced by the backroom boys to chose between Clinton and
Powell.

Or what about a choice between two women, Clinton vs Dole?

It all stimulates the mental processes, doesn't it?
```

```
US carrier group.  He can also order a laser or GPS guided bomb
through any particular window in the world from stealth aircraft.
```

```
It is that your statement is false; the U.S. has no desire to rule the world.
If we had wanted to do that, we could have done so right after WWII,
and nobody could have stopped us.  What we did was disarm so rapidly
that we had decreased our aircraft carriers from 30 in '45 to 3 in '51,
when the Korean war started.  Remember, Korea was a U.N. action.
Unfortunately, the rest of the world sat back and provided only trivial
resources toward that conflict.

It is in the U.S. national interest (and everyone else's) for there to be
world peace.  Unhappy as we are about it, the U.S. is sometimes drawn
into situations (e.g. Bosnia) simply because the rest of the world sits
back whining about the problem, without the guts and determination to
*do* anything about it.  Europe should have handled Bosnia without
our intervention, but they wouldn't do it.  Through inaction we failed to
stop the Holocaust, and we don't want anything like that to happen again.
Unfortunately, the world is too big for one country to completely stop
things like Bosnia and Rwanda.  To interpret U.S. actions in Bosnia,
Iraq/Kuwait, Somalia, Afghanistan, etc. as wanting to 'rule the world'
denotes a truly vast misunderstanding of what the U.S. is all about.
```

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote 
> Ahhhhh, the old songs... *look* for, the *u*nion *la*bel, when you are

> DD

In the late fifties-early sixties (there were 2 editions), there was a
small book called "The Bosses' Songbook".  It was actually a parody of
"The People's Songbook". which Pete Seeger had edited.

I remember a few excerpts:

"A miners life is soft and plushy
In his air-conditioned home.
He has lovely hours and wages,
Yet he'll grumble and he'll groan.
Persecute their organizers!
Throw their leaders into jail!
Get their fingers off the dollar!
Readjust your crooked scale!

Operators stand together,
Heed no organizer's tale!
Get their fingers off the dollar!
Readjust your crooked scale!"


There was an anarchist parody of which I can remember only 2 lines:

"Destruction's a creative urge!
Have you ever seen a wheatfield burn?"



------------
and from the Vietnam era, for the folks who opposed the US course of
war,
but didn't quite trust the Viet Cong:

"And before I'll be a slave.
I'll be buried in my grave,
Or go North with Ho Chi Minh and be free.
Relatively....
```

```
Stephen J Spiro wrote:
> docdwarf@panix.com wrote 
> 
…[55 more quoted lines elided]…
> Relatively....

Reminds me of:

Talking Vietnam Potluck Blues
Words and Music by Tom Paxton

When I landed in Vietnam,
I hardly got to see Saigon.
They shaped us up and called the roll,
And off we went on a long patrol.
Swattin' flies, swappin' lies,
Firing the odd shot here and there.

The captain called a halt that night
And we had chow by the pale moonlight.
A lovely dinner they planned for us
With a taste like a seat on a crosstown bus.
Some of the veterans left theirs in the cans
For the Viet Cong to find. . .
Deadlier than a land mine.

Well naturally somebody told a joke
And a couple of fellas began to smoke.
I took a whiff as a cloud rolled by
And my nose went up like an infield fly.
The captain, this blonde fella from Yale, said
"What's the matter with you, baby?"

Well, I may be crazy, but I think not.
I'd swear to God that I smell pot.
But who'd have pot in Vietnam?
He said, "What do you think you're sittin' on?"
These funny little plants, thousands of them.
Good God Almighty... Pastures of Plenty!

We all lit up and by and by
The whole platoon was flying high.
With a beautiful smile on the captain's face
He smelled like midnight on St. Mark's Place.
Cleaning his weapon, chanting the Hare Krishna.

The moment came as it comes to al,
When I had to answer nature's call.
I was stumbling around in a beautiful haze
When I met a little cat in black P.J.'s,
Rifle, ammo-belt, B.F. Goodrich sandals.
He looked up at me and said,
"Whatsa' matta wit-choo, baby?"

He said, "We're campin' down the pass
And smelled you people blowin' grass,
And since by the smell you're smokin' trash
I brought you a taste of a special stash
Straight from Uncle Ho's victory garden.
We call it Hanoi gold."

So his squad and my squad settled down
And passed some lovely stuff around.
All too soon it was time to go.
The captain got on the radio. . .
"Hello, headquarters. We have met the enemy
And they have been smashed!"

*** Note. Tom has recorded this several times and the exact lyrics have 
changed slightly each time. The version above is (except for the word 
"funny" in verse 4) from the music book "Tom Paxton Anthology" published 
in 1971.
```

```
"Liam Devlin" <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net> wrote:
>
> Good example, doc, why not finish up by describing the Triangle
> Shirtwaist fire,

That was about pure greed, Americans were incensed to learn that
such practices existed, and that situation has been fixed for a long
time.  There is even a department of the U.S. Government whose
prime directive is to enforce safety in the workplace.

> or the court's affirming a child's "right" to work 7 days per week,

That wasn't about the court being insensitive to children. It was
about one of the fundamental principles upon which this country
was founded, that people should be as free from government
interference as possible. This was because most early American
settlers fled tyrannical governments in Europe and the rest of the
world. From the beginning of time until only recently, parents
were given complete authority over their young children, and in
most of the rest of the world parents had the authority to even kill
their children at will.  This practice still goes on in some parts of
the world today. It has never been legal to kill one's children here,
except by abortion, and that has only been legal for a few decades.

> or of Ford's goon squads firing live ammunition into
> striking workers in the 30's because old Henry hated unions?

Henry Ford had some serious problems, greed and anti-Semitism
prominent among them.  Neither attitude is unique to America, the last
time I read any recent world history.  There have never been millions
(or even dozens) rounded up to be killed here, as it has in many other
places, including Europe, in living memory.

But in Europe the practice of the local ruler spending the wedding
night with all new brides was a common practice at one time.  There
was even a name for that practice, but I can't recall it at the moment.
Except for a period in the Roman empire, only since the U.S. was
formed has there been government where the people can appoint and
remove their rulers legally and non-violently.  Even though it is only
226 years old, the U.S. government is the oldest existing government
in the world.  No wealthy person in America has ever had the power
of heriditary or other monarchs that existed in most of the rest of the
world until after the U.S. government was formed.  It was the U.S. who
showed the way for the rest of the world (the papers in Europe said at
the time George Washington was to leave the Presidency that "blood
will run in the streets" because no one in Europe ever gave up their
power peacefully), and all free governments today were modeled, at
least in part, on the U.S. system, which took some things from Rome,
of course.  If you are looking for a pattern of opression of the masses,
there are far more severe models from recent history throughout the rest
of the world.

> Anyone not in the moneyed ruling class has had to struggle dammned
> hard to get anywhere in the US.

There is no 'moneyed ruling class' here. A wealthy person has always
had more influence than a poor one, in every place and every time
throughout history. The U.S. is no different in that respect from other
nations. The difference is that here poor people can become wealthy
through talent and hard work easier than anywhere else in the world.
I grew up on a farm, and make well into 6 figures annually. I did have
to work pretty hard, but no harder than most other folks. I certainly
haven't suffered to any great extent in the process, or trampled others
to get here. Henry Ford came from very poor beginnings.  If you took
the 100 most wealthy people in America, probably most of them made
it themselves from poor beginnings, and many of them have no more
than a high school education, some not even that.  A lot of them were
immigrants who left Europe and other places because it was easier to
'make it' here than anywhere else. There are more wealthy people per
capita in the U.S. than any other place, simply because it *is* easier
to 'make it' here than anywhere else. During the boom in the 90's many
common laborers, such as assembly line workers, became millionaires
through company stock options. In other words, that so called 'wealthy
ruling class' provided common working people an environment in
which *they* could become wealthy too. That's the sort of 'real
oppression' and 'keeping the commoners down' that you find here. The
facts above are well documented, not fiction like the U.S. having a
'wealthy ruling class'. :-)
```

```
"Donald Tees" <donald_tees@sympatico.ca> wrote:
>
> Also called the royal perogative.  Most of the recent US presidents have
> believed in the tradition, but it went out in the rest of the world a
> century or two back.

Well, if you say 'Democratic Presidents' I might agree with you. :-)

The practice dates back to FDR at least, who was infamous for it,
among the Whitehouse press corps (not that any of it reached the
public then, of course). And he in a wheelchair too. Eleanor was a
closet lesbian and kept a live-in lover at the Whitehouse. I've seen
her picture, but forget her name. JFK was probably the horniest.
He often commented "If I go two or three days with having a
woman, I  get headaches." Many heads of state knew about this and
took measures to accommodate his passions when he visited. I heard
one person, who had been part of the Whitehouse press corps at the
time, say that JFK was extremely blunt about it. He apparently
would be introduced to some attractive woman, and then ask her
point blank "Do you want to  <four letter word>?" Once Jackie left
the Whitehouse for a trip. Before her limo reached the Whitehouse
gate, JFK was already skinny dipping with a number of quite naked
young 'ladies'. Jackie had forgotten something, and when her limo
turned around, the Secret Service had to quickly notify JFK and get
the girls out of sight. LBJ was probably the crudest, though a close
second to JFK in appetite. He was fond of asking women "How
would you like to <same four letter word> the President of the
United States?" He also liked to flatulate openly and loudly in his
office with visitors present, then laugh his head off. Everybody
already knows about Bill Clinton, of course. As far as I know,
Jimmy Carter was straight-laced about such things. Stupid, but not
immoral.

I'll have to admit, I can understand how people in other countries
could look at these men and get a very poor impression of the U.S.

The only Republican President I know of in recent times who had an
adulterous affair (though he wasn't President at the time) was Ike. He
had an affair with his driver while in England during WWII.  I'm not
aware of Ike running around other than that.
```

```
It's worth reflecting how this series of threads started. Somebody in the
States saying "The US President is the most powerful person in the World".
Richard countered, "If he comes to Kiwiland he is just another visitor".

Then it becomes all too clear that for many Americans, the interpretation of
power means MILITARY POWER, sharply followed by ECONOMIC POWER (success
stories like Microsoft, Enron, Worldcom etc.- what could possibly be wrong
with monopolies or shafting people from their savings). How about another
avenue, "Power is the art of persuasion", accepting that Military Power, with
justifiable circumstances, is a last resort. The Power of Persuasion is more
likely to supply those friends that you seem to desperately need.

Jimmy
```

```
showed very little patience with those who knowingly twisted the truth,

> > No. Only the most powerful office in the US. If he comes over here he
> > is just another visitor.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…

Notice the wink at the end? Means I wrote that in jest. But like it or not,
the U.S. President does have enormous power, military, financial and
otherwise, and he is not simply a powerless guest, as you or I would be.
But neither is the head of any nation, for that matter. People from other
countries appear to be so sensitive about America's power, they seem
incapable of interpreting anything an American says, other than as
braggadocio or bullying, then use that unvarying and unfair evaluation
as proof that we *are* that way. This is circular logic. The business of
U.S. foreign policy is, first and foremost, to protect and promote U.S.
interests, just as for any other country. When other countries do this,
you call it 'politics', but when America does it, you call it 'being self
centric' or 'bullying', simply because the U.S. is powerful and wields a
lot of influence, which makes us harder to ignore.  It was not the U.S.
who blew the bottom out of a Greenpeace ship several years ago, but I
have not heard anyone here expressing how France is a 'bully'.
```

```
was elected in 1980, the whole country did an about face, morale wise.

it did), that speaks volumes for how Americans perceived Jimmy Carter.
Carter is a good man, but not cut out to be President of the United States.
```

```
"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:
>
> Judson McClendon wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> twenty years ago.

I don't think Carter is stupid; naive would be a better word. IIRC, his
degree is in physics, not a degree someone truly stupid is likely to
pursue or attain. But all of us know of people who are academically
brilliant, but in practical matters can't even tie their shoes. I know and
have known many such people and can spot them a mile away. To my
eye, Jimmy Carter fits this stereotype perfectly, though certainly not an
extreme case. For example, Carter has the same blindness that Neville
Chamberlain had; he simply cannot understand that some people are
just plain evil, and like mad dogs, you have but three options: avoid
them if possible, lock them up, or destroy them. You cannot reason
with them, no matter how long and hard you try. People who have this
kind of blindness aren't bad people, quite the opposite. They are good
and reasonable, and just can't conceive how people could be evil and
unreasonable. But it can be a deadly flaw in a national leader (as it
was in Chamberlain), because people like Hitler, Stalin and Sadam
do exist and must be dealt with, like it or not. If the European leaders
of the time had had enough insight to read by his actions exactly what
Hitler was about, Hitler could have been dealt with a lot earlier, and
the world would have been spared vast suffering and destruction.
```

```
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> seems that human behavior is rarely clear, simple and black-and-white.

This is true. But if you know somebody for 20 years and *never* see
or hear about them telling a lie, you can have high confidence that they
are truthful. On the other hand, if you know somebody five minutes and
catch them in a lie, you can have high confidence they will lie to you
again. If you want leaders who will represent the people honestly,
instead of their own personal interests, then integrity is paramount. A
very competent criminal is still a criminal. Integrity *does* matter.
```

```
I guess that when it comes to "judging" presidents,  I consider a "vow" of
   "read my lips - no new taxes"
to be a more relevant vow than a marriage vow (which depending upon religion
or legislative form may OR MAY NOT say anything implicitly or explicitly
about monogamy)

Anyone who thinks that Hillary Clinton or Jacquie Kennedy *thought* that
their husbands were monogamous by the time they were elected president, has
a very different analysis of those women than I do.  (I don't know about
Mamie Eisenhower - nor do I know what Ike did AFTER he was elected).
Eleanor Roosevelt wasn't even sleeping in the same bedroom with Franklin by
the time he was elected (for the first time) and it is well known why and
when this started.

When judging (well after the fact) various presidents, you really do need to
evaluate DIFFERENT parts of their lives and presidencies.  Certainly LBJ's
record on Civil Rights versus his Viet Nam record were quite different.
Even "Tricky Dickie" did LOTS of good things for the country - while
bringing the presidency (and the entire US) to one of its lowest levels of
prestige. I just don't see that much difference in judging CANDIDATES for
the office.  (I personally would be MUCH happier with a president with
"affairs" than one who held Tipper Gore's views on the 1st amendment and the
entertainment industry - luckily for me, her husband "supported" her, but
didn't seem quite so "unconstitutional" in his views)

P.S. If anyone doubted it before,
  Yes, I am a registered Democratic (who occasionally votes for Libertarian
candidates)
     Yes, I am a "card carrying" member of the ACLU
       But, yes, I am an active member of a NON-fundamentalist Christian
Denomination
            And
   Yes, I have no idea what any of this or similar threads have to do with
COBOL or programming.
```

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com> wrote:
>
> When judging (well after the fact) various presidents, you really do need
> to evaluate DIFFERENT parts of their lives and presidencies.

Of course. I'm not suggesting that marital fidelity be the *only* criterion,
I am saying that it is a very important one. It is a fact of human nature that
people are not completely honest in most areas of their lives, but dishonest
in some parts. Eventually, dishonesty will spread throughout their lives.
If one is willing to lie about one important issue, you can bet the bankroll
that they will lie about other issues too. And if they fail to uphold one major
responsibility, you can be sure they will fail at others too. This is not just
my opinion, it is a clearly understood fact of human nature. Yes, there are
very few people who have completely compartmentalized lives, but those
people have serious mental disorders, such as multiple personalities. No
normal person is compartmentalized to that degree.

> Yes, I have no idea what any of this or similar threads have to do with
> COBOL or programming.

That's why the subject of the thread starts with 'OT'. ;-)
```

```
"Liam Devlin" <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net> wrote:
>
> How about a divorced person as president? RR was the first.


Divorce is a different animal. Just because a person is divorced does
not mean they are unfaithful. I was divorced because my first wife, of
almost 20 years, divorced me 14 years ago. I was never unfaithful to
her, and did not want a divorce, but I had no choice. She wanted the
freedom to sleep with anyone she liked, and has been doing a pretty
good job of that, according to our children (of whom I got custody).
Though I am by no means a perfect person or husband, that divorce
was not about me, but something inside of her (she has what is called
"borderline personality disorder"), which anyone who knows both
of us (except her parents, but including our children) will tell you. I
was good to her, I did not neglect her, I never threw clothes in the
floor or made messes she had to clean up, I was always happy to talk
with her, gave her plenty of affection, and because I own my own
company was able to work at home (she complained about that). I
was a good and faithful husband, provider and father for almost 20
years, and was a far better (kinder, gentler, wiser, wealthier, more
patient) person when she divorced me than when she married me. I
know nobody cares about this, but I say it to illustrate a point.  You
cannot judge that, simply because someone is divorced, they have
failed to be faithful, keep commitments, or even be a good marriage
partner. Only when you know the true details of a person's conduct
in a marriage, and how and why the divorce came about, can you
make any judgment about someone who is divorced.
```

- **References:** `<ak4c8s$1f0$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <jVza9.5996$p%3.454322@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <vu5b9.4338$z21.1048122@news20.bellglobal.com> <akipnm$7c6$1@panix1.panix.com> <17ib9.10558$XV.352498@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net> <kFnb9.44948$Hd4.7815190@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com> <EVoIvBA3dhb9EwkN@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> <w%pb9.6901$uF.1065317@news20.bellglobal.com> <LJrb9.93160$%v4.5129886@e3500-atl2.usenetserver.com> <64Fb9.4159$D02.43141@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net> <cyLb9.61363$Hd4.8734349@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com> <7rOb9.8005$uF.1333578@news20.bellglobal.com> <loQb9.62725$Hd4.9465544@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com>`

```
Judson's comment below made me go and "look" at what some wedding vows
state.  According to
  http://www.documentsanddesigns.com/verse/traditional_wedding_vows.htm#4

The (Episcopal) Book of Common Prayer (and I think this is the "old" one -
not current ones) has,

"I, (name) take thee, (name) to be my wedded (husband/wife),
to have and to hold from this day forward,
for better, for worse, for richer, for poorer,
in sickness or in health, to love and to cherish, '
til death do us part,
according to God's holy ordinance;
thereto I plight thee my troth. (Thereto
I pledge my love)."

I don't see even a HINT that this says that this is to the EXCLUSION of
"loving" anyone else (much less "making love" to someone else - which may
well be an entirely different thing).

This does not say that
  A) most couples don't explicitly or implicitly make such a commitment
  B) That both old and new Testaments don't make statements on this subject.
(Assuming a Judeo-Christian environment)

On the other hand, it DOES go to the question of exactly WHAT vows a
president (or anyone else) has or has not "broken" if they are not
"faithful" (physically) to their spouse.

Although that web page includes a BUNCH of other vows, I am curious (as
someone who has never been in a "committed" relationship) exactly WHAT vows
you (Judson) or others made that EXPLICITLY said they would be physically
(or even emotionally) monogamous - which may or may not be the same as
"faithful" depending upon your view of whether love of one person excludes
it for others.
```

```
> live?" (The response, of course, is "I will".)

But even here, most people believe there are conditions in which you can
abrogate this vow.
```

```
Depends doesn't it? If someone is threatening you at gunpoint and you promise to not tell the police,
do you think it is morally reprehensible to NOT keep that promise? I certainly don't.

Marriage is, like everything else worth doing, a LOT of work. Some relationships are harder to make
work than others, and some people just fail at. Seems pretty high handed to me to judge them about it.

But then again, the relationships I have had have always been based upon mutual respect and
recognition that the other person is an equal partner.  The last 21 years have been in an exclusive
marital state, but even if we had never married, I don't think our relationship would be significantly
different. Karen, of course, might feel differently. <grin>

-Paul
```

```
choice.   Protecting your children is not "moral relativism run amok".
```

```
I am suggesting that it is a private matter between the parties involved.
The fact that you decide someone else's marriage is "nothing at all" is

>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

You know nothing of the sort.  You are quite willing to judge someone else a
liar and oath-breaker, based on the fact that they did not keep the sort of
vow that *you* made to *your* wife.

*** hiatus ***

I use to have a father-in-law that was, to my mind, a narrow minded bigot. I
even liked the man, to an extent.  I never trusted him, although I could
just about always predict his reaction.

He was a completely fair man, honest in ever respect, and expected nothing
of anybody that he did not expect of himself.  He dealt with every single
person, regardless of race, religion, etc., the same.  Why do I think he was
a bigot?  Because his definition of "fair" included keeping all the rules,
AND it was completely inconceivable to him that other people might have
*different* rules.  He could never accept that ... he was *completely* fair,
as long as *he* got to choose the rules.

Why did I never trust him?  Because *my* rules, and *my* beliefs were
different, and I could always be equally assured that those rules and those
beliefs would be given no credance at all when he judged.  He would judge
everyone by his rules, and no one by their own.

I do not mean to be offensive, Judson, and I have listened very carefully to
your reponses.  You accusations of having a hair up my ass regarding you and
your government are noted, and I even agree with you.  I am pushing hard.
But it truly is an attempt to let you see where I am coming from.

If you start with the premise that only one value system is valid, then you
cannot possibly be fair, nor can you be just.  It is a false premise.
Different people believe different things.  A just and fair person *has* to
try to see and understand other value systems.  I think you try to see other
viewpoints, because part of your own value system include the words "fair",
and "just".  I get the impression, however, that it is lip service, that you
are completely convinced of your absolute correctness in all moral/ethical
judgements, and have a deeply seated belief that anything else is simply
"wrong".  Not "different", but "wrong".

Donald
```

```
Well, I've already expressed my skepticism as to 'organized' charities.

quite true. I give to two only (1) Salvation Army - which *is* a religious
organization, but makes no attempt to push religion down your throat - and

right-wing,  but is also acknowledged, from national surveys,  as the
'Charity capital' of Canada.
```

```
his right ! Even given the culture back then, (i.e. women were inferior),
perchance the odd 'knight' was a happy family man and doting father, content
with getting his jollies with his legal wife ?

>
> Except for a period in the Roman empire, only since the U.S. was
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> in the world.

Look a little closer at English history. Magna Carta - not the source of
'democracy' but the robber barons telling King John to piss off. Gradually
the middle merchant class came to have more prominence - the House of
Commons - and weren't an easy pushover when a king wanted more money for a
foreign expedition. The monarchy countered by introducing the House of
Lords, filled with their henchmen, who by royal 'grace and favour' owned
huge tracts of land.

Although still not formulated as we know it today, the pattern for the
Commons was fairly well settled during the Tudor era of Henry VIII and his
daughter Elizabeth I. While still absolute monarchs they  had to cozy up to
the Commons when they wanted funding.

This is what James VI(Scotland)/James I(England) inherited. His daft son,
Charles I, thought he could play the monarchist game. The Commons and Oliver
Cromwell's roundheads told him otherwise - and he lost is head at Whitehall
for his stubbornness.
From then on in, ignoring the minor blip of them discarding James II, a
convert to Catholicism, (Charles I's second son), the pattern was firmly
established.

> No wealthy person in America has ever had the power
> of heriditary or other monarchs that existed in most of the rest of the
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> through talent and hard work easier than anywhere else in the world.

The 'Great American Dream'. Bullshit. Go tell 'em that in Harlem. As you
point out, as in your own case, it can *be* done, (and all credit to you - I
wouldn't mind a piece of your six figures <G>), - but it is still largely,
'being in the right place at the right time", and given the millions of
folks you have, that just doesn't apply to the majority - not everybody can
up roots and go seeking a California dream.. What is the current count -
some 40 million without health coverage of any sort.  Don't counter with
'schemes' that partially alleviate this. Let me quote you a solid example -
both my wife and I independently underwent surgery, me two weeks ago. Do you
know what was the most expensive part of my wife's hospital stay ?
The parking fee I had to pay to visit the hospital, plus the daily rental
for a TV  - the rest cost us ZILCH 1

>
```

```
is handing out prosperity for sitting on your butt. The opportunity is there.
Some are willing to work for it, and some had rather be in a street gang.

> point out, as in your own case, it can *be* done, (and all credit to you - I
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> some 40 million without health coverage of any sort.

I'm one of that 40 million. Health insurance is so expensive and hard to get
for an individual, that it simply isn't worth it.

Who says that everyone had a *right* to health insurance? Do you have
a *right* to a new car every year, or a *right* to a home or a TV? You

> > I grew up on a farm, and make well into 6 figures annually. I did have
> > to work pretty hard, but no harder than most other folks. I certainly
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> What's your basis for saying that. Which parts of the Roman system.

Primarily democracy, the election of leaders by the population.

> Which parts of the US system - two Houses - now doesn't that sound vaguely
> familiar from what I've written above.

If I recall my history correctly, England had a heriditary monarchy when
the U.S. was formed. And unlike today, the king had real power then. Are
you saying that England was a constitutional democracy in 1776?

> Are you suggesting one of the most populous Third World democracies,
> India, has taken its system from  the US system?

You are mistaking what I meant. I did not mean that they 'copied' our
system, I mean that it was the U.S. who proved that a democracy could
really work. At the time (1776), our government was called 'The Great
Experiment" by most of the rest of the world. The great prosperity and
freedom reaped by America then encouraged other countries to establish
democracies of their own.

Actually, the U.S. is a republic, not a pure democracy. That means the
people elect representatives (called 'electors'), who go to Washington
and cast their votes for the candidate of their choice. This made sense in
1776, because of slow travel and communications. It also explains why
a President can be elected, yet have less actual public votes than another
candidate. If a candidate just barely wins a state, he gets *all* the state
electoral votes. Theoretically, a winning candidate could win a lot of
electoral votes by very close margins in the states he carried, while his
opponent could win fewer states, but win all of  hem by a wide margin,
giving the loser many more popular votes, yet losing the election. IIRC,
there have been two presidential elections where the winner received
less popular votes, but in both cases the popular vote was very close.
Today, the electors are sworn to vote for a particular candidate, and the
ballot shows the name of the candidate, not the elector, as it used to do.
```

```
"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:
>
> I didn't use the word RIGHT, you introduced it. Since time immemorial small
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> when necessary, fair rules in commerce and the overall health of the populace.

Your definition of government and mine are different. I see government's
primary responsibility as protecting the people from harm, and providing
certain necessary infrastructure that would not or could not be done by
private enterprise (e.g. mail, roads, police, military). But not to ensure the
welfare of the people individually. That responsibility is the individual's,
assisted by family, church and other private resources. Government should
protect innocent people from crime, defend the country from aggression.
Essentially, to provide an environment where people can live safely and
freely. But I do not believe it is the job of government to ensure the success
of any individual. The people should not expect toe government to guarantee
cradle-to-the-grave security from their own failure. Government is not God.
Government doesn't have infinite resources, and is led by fallible people.
No government will ever have enough money to do it. My definition is very
close to what the framers of the U.S. Constitution had in mind. If we examine
your concept of government by reducto ad absurdum we wind up with pure
socialism. As we have seen illustrated very clearly in recent history,
socialism does not work.  There must be reward for productive effort and
penalty for slothfulness, or a culture will stagnate and die, as surely as the
sun rises. Even China recognizes this, though it had to be drilled into the
very hard heads of the Communist leadership by a hard reality, before they
begrudgingly accepted it.

If you examine the statistics, you will find that local religious charitable
organizations (not huge ones) are orders of magnitude more effective
and efficient than government run organizations to do the same thing. The
difference is so dramatic that people who would never have accepted it
a while back are now beginning to suggest that government funds for
charitable purposes would be better distributed by local religious groups.
I don't particularly like the idea myself, I would rather see taxes reduced
by that amount and let the people handle it. In the U.S., for the government
to put one dollar into the hands of a recipient, it has to collect ten dollars
in taxes. Government should do what only government can do, then leave
the rest to private enterprize.
```

```
small village, north of Portsmouth island, christened Porchester by the Romans,
subsequently used by Henry V to embark on his attack on France, and then from Tudor
times onwards Portsmouth itself was the focus. It was the  home of Nelson's 1805
fleet and was one of the large naval bases during WW1 and WW2 along with Plymouth and
Chatham. The infrastructure was that a large proportion of the population supported
the Navy in the dockyard, (akin to Seattle and Boeing) - now the dockyard no longer
```

- **References:** `<ak4c8s$1f0$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <jVza9.5996$p%3.454322@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <vu5b9.4338$z21.1048122@news20.bellglobal.com> <akipnm$7c6$1@panix1.panix.com> <17ib9.10558$XV.352498@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net> <kFnb9.44948$Hd4.7815190@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com> <HOjc9.682$XQ1.2630@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net>`

```
"Liam Devlin" <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net> wrote:
> Judson McClendon wrote:
> > "Liam Devlin" <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> Your post really whitewashes this situation/affair.


Believe me, I have no intention of whitewashing the Triangle factory
fire. It was one of the most horrible examples of greed and human
callousness that I have ever heard.

I am not against government at all, it is an absolutely necessary part
of any complex society. But a government has a proper role, and that
does not include insuring an individual's success. Government's place
is to provide an environment where people can be free and have
protection from others intentionally or negligently harming them or
their property. Protecting people from unsafe working conditions is
a proper function of government, because if management is negligent
there, it can cause harm to someone. Just as negligence in driving a
vehicle can harm someone.

Let me give a common example of government overstepping its role.
Laws requiring seatbelts or other restraints for children is a proper
law, because it protects the innocent who cannot protect themselves.
But laws requiring a responsible adult to wear seatbelts oversteps
the line, because a person has the right to hang glide from a 1000'
cliff, or sky dive, jump off a bridge with an elastic band strapped to
their ankle, or any other pursuit that has danger, so long as it does not
endanger others. Not wearing a seatbelt (I have always used them)
endangers only the individual who chooses not to wear it. (If it can
be shown that a driver not wearing a seatbelt imposes more risk on
others, then adult seat belt laws are justified.) If we can logically
require seatbelts for responsible adults, then what logic says we
should not also outlaw those even more dangerous pursuits above?
The criterion should always be "protect the innocent from harm by
others, and protect those not responsible (e.g. children, mentally
impaired) from harm by others or themselves", but *not* "protect
responsible adults from risk of their own choosing" and *not* "make
sure everyone is well and successful." BTW laws prohibiting suicide
are justified for two reasons, that one who tries to take his own life is
not rational (protect those not responsible), and protect insurance
companies (protect the innocent shareholders) from being defrauded.
```

```
"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:
>
> Judson McClendon wrote:
…[34 more quoted lines elided]…
> infringement on personal rights ? They are not affecting others ?

I am not aware of any claim ever made that not wearing a seatbelt endangers
any person other than onesself. Of course, any time one is seriously injured
or killed, it affects their family and loved ones. But exactly the same can be
said for sky diving, etc.

> Statistically we know you create greater 'danger' to yourself not wearing a
> seat belt.

Statistically, you create a greater danger to yourself by jumping off a 1000'
high bridge with an elastic band tied to your foot too.  So?  Should we
outlaw that on the same premise?

> And back to the other topic, chances are they become short to
> long-term stayers in the health system, and can even finish up as
> paraplegics. The 'preventive medicine' here is make all wear seat belts.

Once again, the same argument can be made for other hazerdous activities,
such as 'extreme' sports. There simply is no logical rationale in forcing
responsible adults to wear seatbelts while permitting them to do far more
dangerous non productive activities like extreme sports.

> I can get no more upset about seat belt legislation than I can supporting a
> government which wants to introduce national id cards as a preventive against
> criminality, terrorism or espionage. Phooey to the 'Big Brother' fear in our
> western democracies.

Well, I was using seatbelt laws as a clear and logically indefensible example
of government overstepping it's proper function, not as a tirade against them.

> So legislate against suicide, "protect those not responsible", but don't go
> for universal health care to 'protect those less fortunate' - the have-nots
> who dropped through the cracks in the system ?

You miss the point, Jimmy.  National health care *forces you by threat of
imprisonment* to *support somebody else's health*.  Even if they choose
to risk their health and life by doing foolish and dangerous things, *you*
must pay to provide them health care.  You should be responsible for what
*you do*, not for what *someone else does*.  If your neighbor smokes in
bed and sets his house on fire, should you be forced to pay for it?  You are
free to help them if you want to, but the government should not *force* you
to do so. There is *absolutely no* logical defensible distinction between
one and the other.

When the government taxes you to provide roads or police or military, it
is to *your* benefit.  Even if you never use the road, commerce uses it to
provide you with products and services that *you* need.  But government
*should not* force one person to pay for the personal welfare of another.

You have no control over what another person does, and you should not
be held hostage to their responsible behavior or lack of it.  Responsibility
and authority go together.  If you have authority over it, then you are also
responsible for it.  Making someone responsible without also giving them
the authority is *always* unfair.  Parents are often held legally responsible
for what their non adult children do, *because* the parents have authority
over the children. We live in a causative universe, and there are *rules*
(natural laws) by which things operate. Things don't just 'happen' for no
reason, and we should not do things for no reason.  We should do or not
do things because there is a *principle* that supports it.  An airplane will
not fly because it "looks like a good idea", it will fly when it satisfies the
laws of physics that apply.  Neither will social actions work "because
they look like a good idea" either.  They will work when they satisfy the
laws (principles) that apply there too.  One of the *absolutely fundamental*
laws of social behavior, which can be easily demonstrated, conceptually
and in practice, is *authority and responsibility go together*.

There is a good reason why "taxation without representation is tyranny."
It is responsibility without authority.  There is a good reason why IRS
(U.S. income tax) agents frequently abuse their power.  It is authority
without responsibility (accountability).  A person must be held accountable
for their own actions, or you have anarchy; and must not be held accountable
for the actions of others without authority over the others, or you have
tyranny.  This is a natural social law, not opinion.  Like other natural laws,
you follow it and you succeed, you break it and you fail.  Opinion makes
not one whit of difference.  :-)
```

- **References:** `<ak4c8s$1f0$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <jVza9.5996$p%3.454322@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <vu5b9.4338$z21.1048122@news20.bellglobal.com> <akipnm$7c6$1@panix1.panix.com> <17ib9.10558$XV.352498@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net> <kFnb9.44948$Hd4.7815190@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com> <HOjc9.682$XQ1.2630@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net> <vlpc9.20545$FJ1.3058409@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com> <3D727DFD.64712FEA@shaw.ca> <b3Qc9.7235$jG2.526722@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <3D73DA19.D46CE39D@shaw.ca> <SeVc9.54716$vY2.1327338@e3500-atl2.usenetserver.com> <ZpzG4UNLyRNq-pn2-NdrX5g2XSbGj@h24-82-204-17.wp.shawcable.net>`

```
<lsunley@mb.sympatico.ca> wrote:
> Judson McClendon <jud@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
> >
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> providing the health care services.

You are correct, the problem with current free enterprise medical care
is that there is no accountability *to the consumer*.  Accountability to the
consumer is absolutely essential for free market forces to work.  What
has removed the consumer from the loop is two things: health insurance
provided by the government and health insurance provided by employers.
I have no problem with health insurance paid by the individual, because
the insurance costs are a feedback that a consumer can understand and
deal with.  If there were no such thing as government or employer health
insurance, you can bet your last cent that health care costs would be
dramatically less than they are now.  I have already addressed government
health care.  But right now, employers pay staggering premiums for health
care for their employees.  How many employees, if given the opportunity,
knowing how much those premiums are, would choose the insurance over
having the money in their pockets?  In this situation, health care providers
would be *forced* to provide better service at lower prices.  Also, people
who had to pay for their own health care would be far less inclined to visit
a doctor when it wasn't really needed, which happens a lot here.  When
the consumer is taken out of the loop, free enterprise won't work, as we
clearly see in the health care business.

This is simply another *fundamental principle* of economics, where no
idea will work because it "looks good", but because it *follows applicable
natural principles (laws)*.  Once again, opinion doesn't matter in the least.
Not mine, not yours.  We live in a universe in which natural principles win
every single time, and opinion doesn't mean spit.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Presidential eligibility.

_(reply depth: 34)_

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2002-09-03T04:19:12+00:00

```
Judson McClendon wrote:

> "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:
> >
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> enterprise does work.

Nothing wrong with free enterprise if it works; something Liam and I touched
on in another message. In principle and in theory you are right on.
Unfortunately, as we find in practice it just doesn't work out that way.

I don't watch because I find the host too abrasive in style, (here we go
again), my Scottish buddy watched the 'McLaughlin Group' on PBS some weeks
ago. Mine host quizzed his four panelists. "Given the troubles we are
having, i.e. Enron, Worldcom etc., are we any worse than the Europeans ?".
Their consensus answer was, "Yes'. And the reasoning for that, 'There is so
much more money involved in the US".

The old 'forties song, "Money is the root of all Evil, ........". The
temptation is so *great* even for the most honest. Wouldn't both you any I
be more than somewhat troubled if somebody offered us $6 million to look the
other way - take the money and run - you hope !

Without even the very mildest government intervention in commerce, there's
no way it can get better, but only worse. Wont be too long before we are
bandying around the term 'trillion' rather than 'billion'; 'million' is
already passï¿½. I no more applaud government intervention than you do, but to
keep potential crooks straight, I just don't see a productive alternative.

Some TV 'talking heads', political scientists, made much of the 'checks and
balances' in your system. That's the theory. Then I think of Tricky Dicky
and Watergate, then Ollie North - just to keep it balanced, have I neglected
to list any Democratic sins (i.e. commercial - not what occurs between the
bed sheets) ?

PS:   If I indicated that Health Care in Canada is free - it isn't.
Initially you pay the federal government through general income tax. Then
the feds and provinces get into a tussle - for example the feds want to pass
out say $6 billion - the provinces then turn around and say but we *need*
$8-9 billion.

Alberta specifically - the province levies a tax on Health Care. For a two
person family  $204 per quarter. Being an old fart, they let me off the
hook. Above that medication has to purchased, (but not during
hospitalization). That $204 above is Alberta Blue Cross coverage - so as for
a recent prescription the bill was $150 - but I only have to pay $50.
Obviously this differs to some extent for each of the other provinces.

Note -  under our very early constitution, (1837), health and education are
in the provincial domain. As also are resources, like our oil/gas - so
Alberta is currently getting very jumpy about environmental issues, and is
waiting on tenterhooks to see what PM Jean Chretien says in S. Africa,
(tomorrow I think), about the Kyoto agreement.

Meanwhile from to-day's paper he was US-bashing. His claim - Canada
subsidizes its farmers to the tune of $4 billion, whereas it is $49 billion
in the US. His conclusion - there's no way we can compete with you.

Jimmy
```

```

On  3-Sep-2002, Liam Devlin <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net> wrote:

> I'm not registered with any party, but I vote for the Libertarian
> candidate when the major parties have absolutely nothing to recommend
> them, e.g., 1980, 1984 & 2000 presidential elections.

In most places, you can remove your party affiliation immediately after an
election, and then pick a party at the primary.  There is a big advantage in
doing this, as you can vote in the primary that offers the biggest danger to
your wants - voting against that danger.   A primary vote counts much, much more
than a general election vote.   I stay registered for a week or so after a
primary, then switch to unregistered, as I don't know ahead of time which party
will have the candidate or position that I will want to vote against.
```
