Rick Smith wrote:

> Go to < http://libertarian.org/ > for discussions of various
> free market issues. Many of the links from this site point to
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> Republican Party and re-registered as a Libertarian.

OK Rick a response to the Health reference. I think we can safely assume the
author is NOT a Libertarian, Liberal or Democrat <G>. That's the first problem,
he is biased - there is no balance in quoting counter sources - his article is
geared to support his own thinking.

So as to his major sources :-

Fraser Institute in Vancouver - ultra, ultra right wing think tank and certainly
to the right of either Ronald Reagan or Maggie Thatcher. These guys must
absolutely LUV the Alberta formulated federal party which started out as the
Reform Party and now call themselves the Canadian Alliance. Unless the federal
Liberals do something truly and *drastically* wrong, the CA doesn't stand a
chance in hell of becoming a national government in the foreseeable future  The
population base, Quebec and Ontario will just *not* accept these western
rednecks !

His other two main sources the National Post and the British Daily Telegraph.
Now I really blow up, although the Telegraph was my favourite reading whilst
living in the UK. We are talking about one of the most arrogant Canadian
bastards I know - Conrad Black. He is head of the Hollinger Group which acquired
most of the Canadian dailies at one stage, including my own Calgary Herald. I've
forgotten names, but the group also owned some 'minor' US papers. The Canadian
group of papers was eventually sold off to Izzy Asper and his son in Manitoba.
(Both are Liberals, without a shadow of a doubt, but under their ownership the
Calgary Herald is still a very CONSERVATIVE newspaper, but for counter-balance
articles are included from the Left). Black is also current owner of the Daily
Telegraph.

"When in Rome do as the Romans do....". I'm a Brit who came to Canada and now I
am a CANUCK. Black sits ensconced in London, and loves nothing better than to
come back and tell us how Canada should be run, courtesy of outfits like the
Fraser Institute. For whatever reason, Tony Blair offered him a peerage, subject
to Canadian approval. Based on precedent, that Canada wants nothing to do with
colonial ties, Chretien rejected the idea. (Max Aitkin became Lord Beaverbrook,
Churchill's sidekick as Minister of War Production. Similarly Lord Thompson of
Fleet Street (newspapers) had to give up his Canadian citizenship to accept his
peerage). Black took the Canadian government to court for its refusal - and
lost. Obviously he must have given up on citizenship because he now has a
peerage and can don his ermine robes so that he can perform in Gilbert and
Sullivan's 'Iolanthe'.

While the owner of the Calgary Herald he broke one of the ethics of newspaper
ownership and used full pages to publish his own ideas. Similarly he gave space
to his wife Barbara Amiel to do the same thing.

Boy do I love this asshole !

Now to the facts in the author's article :-

1 - Fist he takes a swipe at Hillary Clinton, with emotive references like 'a
meeting of Mafia dons" - that's hardly a surprise is it ?
2 - References to using the US Department of Motor Vehicles as parallel to the
Canadian and UK Health systems. I've got news for him. Like the federal Post
Office our Albert Motor Vehicles is privatized - in and out - BUT - you are
still paying into government coffers - they've just off-loaded the clerical
hassle to private enterprise. The 'privates' get a commission on what they do.
3 - As the National Center for Policy Analysis  reported, "[w]aiting lists for
surgery in some  Canadian hospitals can stretch from months  to as long as five
years."  I assume the NCPA to be a conservative site - they quote the Fraser
Institute. We also hear from our press about long delays both in Canada and the
UK. I suppose it largely depends upon the 'local' administration :-

Fact 1 - my wife felt extremely ill after returning from a UK holiday - saw the
family physician May 8, sent her for blood analysis May 8, ultrasound May 16,
saw gynecologist  June 23,  Oncologist June 4, surgery June 11 and home June 18.

Would that have been any quicker with privatization ?.

Fact 2 - Last year's annual medical checkup, my GP asked, "Is that hernia
hurting you ?" Didn't even realize I had one ! This year a hernia pain on the
opposite side of the groin, go see the same family GP July 23, referred
downstairs to the surgeon who will do it July 25, and books me in from his own
time sheet. Visit hospital for pre-op session July 31. Surgery Aug 12, in and
out same day.

Mine was mundane; my wife's was critical.

4- The situation is so bad, says Canada's  National Post, that "[i]n Ontario,
cancer  patients may be asked by their hospital to  sign a waiver acknowledging
the danger of  being forced to wait for treatment."  I don't know, but can't
deny it. It may well be that other jurisdictions haven't been as 'clever' as
Alberta. With an acute shortage of funding Alberta has closed some hospitals,
forced centralization for specialties, medical shopping malls, if you like.
Cancer treatment is like a sausage factory, wheel 'em in and wheel 'em out for
preventive medicine. No choice with the acute, they become long-term stayers.

5- And it's not as if that long wait will be satisfied  by luxury
accommodations. Nope. Once in  the hands of the doctors, you're likely to get
tossed on a gurney, pushed against the wall  of a hospital corridor, and left
there to heal -- or not.

That's a really balanced observation. I guess he's been watching too many US
medical dramas. My wife didn't wait on a gurney, she walked into the operating
room !

6. It's OK, though. The National Post says  there's an alternative for Canadians
in need of  prompt treatment: A bus ticket south. "[I]n  Manitoba, cancer
patients are being shuttled to the United States for therapy."

Can't deny it, we know people go south for some treatments. Plus of course there
is a shortage of specialists and nurses. We train them, but some are greedy
buggers who go south where they can make a bigger buck. Still that's free
enterprise isn't it.
Twenty years ago my wife underwent back surgery. A lady was wheeled into the bed
next to her. She got severe back problems while on vacation in the States. After
a one night stay in a US hospitl she said, "Get me out of here before they
bankrupt me !"

There's also a shortage of things like MRI because of the astronomical price for
such equipment.

7. As in Canada, you shouldn't be ready to  celebrate when you reach the front
of a British  queue. Says Spiers, "[c]ancer care is a  particularly shocking
concern. More than half  of all patients do not see a cancer specialist ...
Hardly surprisingly, UK survival rates are  very poor."

Well on that one, so far as Canada is concerned - my wife's case. However what
logic suggests it would speed up in the UK if it was privatized ? The number of
patients wont diminish - where do the additional doctors suddenly come from ?

I'm not au fait with the UK system as I never had need of it during my first
forty-five years. From memory most hospitals are Victorian edifices, not easily
adaptable to modern administration. Unlike N. America, with nether the space nor
the money, they can't willy nilly pull down and rebuild. The lack of medical
attention through the 19th and early 20th century is well documented in British
literature. Go read the novels of the Scottish A.J Cronin. Several of his novels
were turned into movies.

Back in the early 'thirties an analysis was done showing that Scottish kids were
prone to bone breakages. The source, Scotland's geology is largely granite based
so a lack of calcium in fresh water. Coupled with that there was a dietary
problem associated with the Depression. We are back to the 'have nots'. An early
Labour government introduced free milk for *all* British kids, two free 1/3 pint
bottles of milk per day. That's when the top of the milk also contained cream !
I believe this situation existed until about 1980 - probably dear Maggie
kyboshed it !  An expensive remedy - but PREVENTIVE medicine.

8. He finishes., "OK, I'm sold. I'll avoid the DMV and stick with my private
insurance the next time I get a boo-boo".

Bearing in mind I read a local CONSERVATIVE newspaper, what about situations in
the States where insurance doesn't cover it, or the insurers qualify coverage
because of the illness you have. What about the situations where people have had
to sell their homes to cover medical costs ? None of these are every day events,
but the do occur.

I would have readily accepted his criticisms if he had balanced his paper with
well reasoned free enterprise alternatives, rather than coming on like Attila
the Hun.

Our Canadian health system will never be perfect - but even with its faults
there *is* accessibility.

Jimmy

James J. Gavan <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote in message

>

Every 'political party' has, to those who call themselves
centrist, extremists. Of course the article is biased; but
not so much so that a centrist, like myself <g>, could
totally reject the ideas. For example, he states:

"There are alternatives, though, that move away from
the whole hospital-as-DMV-outpost scenario. Canadian
and American reformers alike are hot for medical savings
accounts, which keep control and responsibility for
healthcare costs in individual hands." and,

"More radical free-market advocates recommend
reversing the current course of health reform by
completely dumping government involvement in
medicine. They argue that government involvement is
the source of most of its problems in the first place.
Therefore, the solution isn't a new government program.
The solution is to get rid of all the old ones."

I am not opposed to government sponsored healthcare;
but, if the government is going to treat healthcare "as a
right" then they should also treat it "like a right." That is,
minimal control by bureaucracy and available to all.
What I have seen is that because government is footing
the bill (with 'our' tax dollars), politicians feel a need to
'control the cost of healthcare' to show they are
responsible to the taxpayer. The effect is that they make
healthcare providers responsible to the government,
instead of responsible to the patients.

Another problem with political control is the result of the
division of the population into privleged groups: Medicare
for those over 65 and Medicaid for the means-tested poor.
Those who are not in these groups pay approximately
thirty percent more than 'fair market value' when using
healthcare services. This came about because the
government 'to control costs' will not pay, from tax dollars,
certain 'overhead' costs, such as training for nurses and
staff. Healthcare providers pass those costs to users.

> Bearing in mind I read a local CONSERVATIVE newspaper, what about
situations in
> the States where insurance doesn't cover it, or the insurers qualify
coverage
> because of the illness you have. What about the situations where people
have had
> to sell their homes to cover medical costs ? None of these are every day
events,
> but the do occur.

These are variable with the individual state and type of
plan. In Florida, I can keep my house regardless of the
amount of medical expenses; except for one circumstance.
If I were to apply to the state's Medicaid program under
conditions where I am unlikely to return to my house; the
state can require the house be sold to satisfy medical
claims before I become eligible for Medicaid. Once I
accept Medicaid payments, the state will place a lien
against the house for satisfaction of Medicaid claims,
after my death. Otherwise, the state does not require the
house to be sold to satisfy other creditors; such as:
hospitals, doctors, etc., even when those costs were
incurred in the same illness for which Medicaid coverage
is later used.

HMOs have restrictions that are intended to allow them
to control costs and mostly the HMOs came about with
government support. Some people did not read the
'fine print' when signing HMO contracts; thereby
restricting the amount and type of their coverage. This
led to a great deal of dissatisfaction with managed
care programs. As I recall, some politicians suggestted
managed care as a model for government sponsored
healthcare. When the problems surfaced, there were,
as I recall, Congressional investigations. <G>
```

James J. Gavan <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote in message
news:3D757979.764894B5@shaw.ca...
[snip]
>
> I agree with both your last paragraphs. Unfortunately, and please correct
me if
> I'm wrong, but I get the impression that people in the States, *all*
political
> affiliations,  generally tend to look upon social medicine as something
evil -
> somebody, somewhere has done a real marketing number on you creating this
bogey
> man - perhaps the medics ?

The American Medical Association was opposed to
Medicare in 1964 and, if I understand correctly, all
forms of managed care that have been proposed.

As I understand it, the principle arguments against
managed care is that it interfers with patients getting
the proper care when it is needed most. This is
because management of healthcare costs means
limiting the options for doctors and patients and by
managing capacities differently than a free market
system might choose.

One example I can give from this past winter is:
One of the local hospitals (there are only 2) had
requested approval to make 14 beds available for
general use. These 14 beds had been approved for
a specialized use, previously. The state disallowed
the request because the hospital was not near
capacity. On the same day the hospital recieved the
disapproval of their request, there was a shortage
of 200 beds for the county. Some patients had to be
moved to hospitals up to 35 miles away. The 14 beds
remained unused while some patients remained on
gurneys in hallways in the ERs.

Since many Canadians winter in this area Florida,
I have no doubt that some have had poor experiences
with medical services.

> Social medicine is indeed expensive, but cut your defence budget in half ?
Can
> somebody supply a current budget figure for total US defence - all three
> services, well four if you want to include the marines <G>.  Now some
additional
> homework - take total US population and divide numbers into one half of
the
> defence budget - what do you get per person ?
>
> It's a wild guess, but I bet the resulting answer is way in excess of what
you
> would need each year for medicare.

I think you are comparing apples and oranges because
Medicare only applies to those 65 and over. The budget
for Medicare probably is less than half the defense
budget; but the comparison needs to be with total
healthcare costs. This translates to approximately
$304B (17% of the U.S. budget outlays) for defense
versus approximately $1500B (14% of U.S. GDP) for
all healthcare costs.
```

James J. Gavan wrote:
> 
> Donald Tees wrote:
> 
(snip)
>>
>>That the people's of a nation feel that the physical health of each and
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> man - perhaps the medics ?

Not everyone in the US believes socialized medicine is the work of the 
devil (or communists). I, and I know I'm not alone, believe it's 
absolutely criminal that the US doesn't have universal health coverage. 
If someone says that's "socialized medicine", I don't care, call it 
"lemon curry" if you want. I think part of the reaction to it reflects 
the US's extreme anti-communist mind set, coupled with the fact that few 
people differentiate between communism and socialism. The American 
Medical Association has lobbied long & hard against anything they 
considered socialized medicine, including Medicare, which, of course, 
they profited from enormously.

For those of us old enough to remember, before Medicare doctors 
routinely spent a half/full day weekly working in the local clinic 
(which was free), plus they based their fees on the patient's ability to 
pay, e.g., my family doctor might have charged my parents $5 for an 
office visit and $10 for someone else who was a business executive or 
lawyer, something like that. I believe medical insurance was rare, the 
first thing I remember hearing about was Blue Cross/Blue Shield. Once 
medical insurance became common and Medicare was in place, doctors 
expect to be paid in full by everyone. Some, of course, abuse any 
system, where there's money, there are always crooks.

Many people have a strong distaste for any government role in anything 
other than the military and giving tax refunds to the rich.

According to an article in the NY Times, the proposed defense budget for 
2002 was something over $300 billion (that's a 1,000 millions), or 52% 
of the entire budget, vs. 10% for Health & Human Services and 7% for 
education.

http://www.sas.upenn.edu/~dludden/USbudget02.htm

> somebody supply a current budget figure for total US defence - all three
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…

```
"Donald Tees" <donald_tees@sympatico.ca> wrote:
>
> That the people's of a nation feel that the physical health of each and
> every individual in that nation is a matter for national concern does not
> strike me as a particularly bad sentiment for a government.

If being a "good sentiment" had anything to do with government programs
working, then every liberal program would be a success, instead of the
reverse, as we see in reality.  Spending more money than you make every
month would look good for a while too.  Following the natural principles
involved is the *only* way any economic program will work.  Government
'gimme' programs *always* strike at the heart of free enterprise: reward
for diligent effort, penalty for sloth.  Anybody who knows more than zip
about the psychology of humans or animals knows that a *consistent*
carrot/stick environment is about the strongest way to mould behavior there
is.

```
"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:
>
> Judson McClendon wrote:
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> Unfortunately, as we find in practice it just doesn't work out that way.

Only because we don't follow the rules, which few people bother to study
and understand, preferring instead to follow what they *think*.  A good
analogy would be somebody trying to bake a cake from a recipe, while
changing the ingrediants and instructions, and when it didn't turn out right,
saying "This recipe is good in theory, but in reality it doesn't work."  ;-)
People used to be entirely ignorant of germs, but their ignorance dit not
make them imune to the slightest degree.  Ignorance of natural law is no
excuse. :-)

> I don't watch because I find the host too abrasive in style, (here we go
> again), my Scottish buddy watched the 'McLaughlin Group' on PBS some weeks
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> much more money involved in the US".

The problem isn't money, the problems are: *greed* and *lack of
integrity*.  Which is why I have been harping about public officials
who commit adultery.  Integrity, or lack of same, is a personal trait,
not something one puts on and takes off like a suit.  When someone
demonstrates a clear lack of integrity in one area, you can bet the
farm they will also show lack of integrity in other areas, eventually
across the board.

> The old 'forties song, "Money is the root of all Evil, ........". The
> temptation is so *great* even for the most honest. Wouldn't both you any I
> be more than somewhat troubled if somebody offered us $6 million to look the
> other way - take the money and run - you hope !

That forties song is a *misquote* of a Bible verse that is frequently
used, but almost always misquoted, and says "For the love of money
is a root of all kinds of evil, for which some have strayed from the
faith in their greediness, and pierced themselves through with many
sorrows." (1 Timothy 6:10, NKJV)  It is the *love* of money, and
greed, which are the evil, not money itself.  Money, like all other
inanimate things, is neither good nor evil.

> Without even the very mildest government intervention in commerce, there's
> no way it can get better, but only worse. Wont be too long before we are
> bandying around the term 'trillion' rather than 'billion'; 'million' is
> already pass�. I no more applaud government intervention than you do, but to
> keep potential crooks straight, I just don't see a productive alternative.

I agree, there is a proper role of government in commercial affairs.
That role is to enforce a fair 'playing field', to the degree that is
possible, and to protect the innocent from financial tyranny.  Laws
prohibiting monopolies and insider trading are examples.

> Some TV 'talking heads', political scientists, made much of the 'checks and
> balances' in your system. That's the theory. Then I think of Tricky Dicky
> and Watergate, then Ollie North - just to keep it balanced, have I neglected
> to list any Democratic sins (i.e. commercial - not what occurs between the
> bed sheets) ?

You are discussing apples and oranges here.  There are financial
'checks and balances', which would work, but are not currently
working because they are not being applied.  For example, we have
crooked politicians because the population, as clearly demonstrated
in these threads, doesn't even understand that integrity is important.
Our financial situation keeps worsening because most people don't
have a clue what the financial laws are.  People get PHDs in finance
from Harvard, and they can quote you hundreds of complex 'rules',
most of which have nothing to do with the natural financial laws.
All natural laws, when properly understood, are relatively simple
to express.  When you can only express a natural law with complex
math, then you don't really understand it yet.

The 'apples' and 'oranges' I referred to above are the 'financial laws',
and the 'social/political laws'.

> PS:   If I indicated that Health Care in Canada is free - it isn't.
> Initially you pay the federal government through general income tax. Then
> the feds and provinces get into a tussle - for example the feds want to pass
> out say $6 billion - the provinces then turn around and say but we *need*
> $8-9 billion.

The figures I have seen show that, for the U.S. government to pay a
recipient $1, they had to have collected $10 in taxes.  The other 90%
is eaten up in beauracracy.  Just from a simple position of efficiency,
even assuming government financed medical care is no more expensive
then privately funded medical care, why would we have to pay *ten
times* as much in taxes as it would cost to buy it ourselves?  This is
what I call 'insanity'.  We see the same thing in welfare.  I don't recall
the specific figures, but someone calculated what the U.S. government
had spent on welfare since the 60's, and it was far and away more than
would have been needed to raise the income level of *all* the poor
people in the U.S. to above the poverty level.  But the majority of it
was squandered, eaten up in beaurocratic waste.  Small, private
charities could have done a far better job with dramatically less.  But
the philosophy "the government will take care of it", induces people
to contribute less than they would, "because I already gave in taxes."
And they do, far more than necessary, to far less benefit.  But as
liberals love to say "We passed laws and spent billions and it only
made matters worse.  The obvious solution is to pass more laws and
spend more billions on the same thing, so it will work."

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Presidential eligibility.

_(reply depth: 36)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com
- **Date:** 2002-09-06T13:36:16-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<alap2g$k5l$1@panix1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<ak4c8s$1f0$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <3D727DFD.64712FEA@shaw.ca> <lJ0e9.22664$%P6.6988384@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com>`

```
In article <lJ0e9.22664$%P6.6988384@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com>,
Judson McClendon <jud@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
>"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:

[snip]

>> The old 'forties song, "Money is the root of all Evil, ........". The
>> temptation is so *great* even for the most honest. Wouldn't both you any I
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>inanimate things, is neither good nor evil.

Ow... do you really want to go down this particular path?  The passage in 
Greek (from Novum Testamentum Graece, Oxford 1910 (1953 reprint)) is 
closer to 'rhizda gar panto(w)n to(w)n kako(w)n estin he philarguria', 
'root for all (of) the bad/evil is the (philarguria)'... and this 
'philarguria' occurs only once in the NT.  It is a word combining one of 
the forms of love (phil-) and the word for silver (similar to the Latin 
'argent').

(side note - 'rhizda' survives in modern English as the... root for 
'rhizome')

'Philos' is the 'mildest' form of love (especially when compared to eros 
and agape); it is sometimes translated as 'friendliness' (as in the 
aphorism 'The things of friends are in common', 'friends' being 'philon') 
and is used in beneficial terms ('philanthropy' = 'phil' + 'anthropos').

Silver and money, on the other hand (pace, gold bugs!) are indicated by 
the same word in other cases, most obviously the Hebrew 'kesef'.

At this point it might be best for all involved to leave this as a bit of 
linguistic trivia... lovely weather, isn't it?

DD
```

- **References:** `<ak4c8s$1f0$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <jVza9.5996$p%3.454322@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net> <vu5b9.4338$z21.1048122@news20.bellglobal.com> <akipnm$7c6$1@panix1.panix.com> <17ib9.10558$XV.352498@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net> <kFnb9.44948$Hd4.7815190@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com> <HOjc9.682$XQ1.2630@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net> <vlpc9.20545$FJ1.3058409@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com> <3D727DFD.64712FEA@shaw.ca> <b3Qc9.7235$jG2.526722@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```

"Russell Styles" <RWSTYLES@worldnet.att.net> wrote in message
news:b3Qc9.7235$jG2.526722@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net...

>     The seat belt problem could be handled by allowing Insurance companies
> to sell policys that have no medical coverage for non wearers.

I agree!

Moreover, for those who insist on the right to Drive Free, insurance
companies should be allowed to offer additional insurance, at (presumably
substantial) extra cost, that would cover medical care in such cases.
Whether they would choose to do so is another story, though.

I don't agree that choosing not to wear seat belts is tantamount to sky
diving in that accidents have no direct victims.  An unrestrained passenger
in a vehicle involved in a serious collision almost certainly becomes a
ricocheting projectile inside the cabin.  The driver has a right to be
protected from projectiles in those cases, and that trumps the passenger's
desire for freedom from restraints.  The passengers in a vehicle have a
right to expect their driver to ensure that he's in close proximity to the
vehicle's controls regardless, as well as the right to the assurance that he
himself won't be a projectile.

Unrestrained drivers and passengers are ejected from vehicles in serious
collisions *far* more frequently than those who were wearing seatbelts, and
people ejected from vehicles stand a*far* greater chance of being a
fataility in such accidents than those who were restrained within them.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```


Liam Devlin wrote:

> Doug Miller wrote:
> > [snip] Anyone not
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> Maybe some posters outside the US can comment on this.

With pleasure. I was going to anyway <G>

I understand where both Doug and Judson are coming from. Their roots are as farm
boys who made good. As Donald responded you can currently do it in any country.

For those of our readers who have not visited N. America it is HUGE. The only ones
likely to get a feel for this are our Aussie friends - if they took a cross country
trek from the west to the east cost of Australia, they would understand what I'm
referring to.

Initially the USA was developed along the East Coast. The more adventuresome and
*courageous* set out in wagons for parts west, gradually colonizing until they
arrived at the west coast. Similarly in Canada, again settled in the east, primarily
what was referred to as Upper and Lower Canada, (what we now refer to as Quebec and
Ontario - but I'm not sure which is the 'Upper' <G>). Later than their US
counterparts, they headed west as a pan-Canadian rail system was built. The
mounties, (then NWMP), first headed for now Fort McLeod in southern Alberta in 1873.
They came up and built Fort Calgary in 1875.

(Side-line trivia - the mountie uniform was typically that of the British military
of the mid Victorian period, scarlet jacket and navy blue trousers. The traditional
'cowboy  hat'  was only introduced when they were used as military police in the
Boer War in S. Africa. That war was also the source for Baden Powell's Boy Scouts
hat).

In both nations - perserverence and diligence allowed many to become 'comfortable',
and even before industrialization there were the very rich. Such a situation for a
'new opportunity' was inconceivable in Europe - there was no place to 'go West' -
the land had already been settled for centuries.

Let's just focus on one European country, Britain, for a comparison of growth,
formulation of politics and individual opportunity. Even if not steeped in British
history you are aware that from the Norman Conquest it was largely an agricultural
base, each villein owing allegiance to his feudal lord, cultivating three strips of
land on a rotating basis. Even back then without modern education, they understood
the necessity of crop rotation. (I recently read a modern agriculturist who says we
are killing the soil with more and more fertilizers. Crops become less, so just keep
on putting in more fertilizers !).

As I said to Judson, democracy was well established in England before the Georgian
era. Although initially a mixed group, the House of Commons became two political
groups Whigs (Liberals aka as Democrats in the US) and Tories (Conservatives aka as
Republicans in the US), the former being the middle merchant class. And my Scottish
buddy, yet again, reminded me tonight that it started out as Whigs and Tories in the
colonies !

Back to my reference to agriculture above. (No wonder I couldn't find a reference
for this - a Google search tells me there were 5,000 Land Enclosure Acts !).
Surnames like Tucker and Weaver were synonymous with sheep rearing. Just so with
Miller. Trace your timeline back Doug, and one of your forebears was a miller of
flour, in a village, using a mill powered probably by a water wheel. Land was
enclosed certainly from Tudor times, sometimes with minor revolts. But probably the
most significant was the Land Enclosures Act of 1750. The landed gentry, successors
to the robber barons, were empowered to enclose what had been common grazing land -
making it impossible for small farmers to survive. Note this dovetails neatly into
the start of the Industrial Revolution. The new factories required a nonexistent
labour force. Where did it come from, those disenfranchised farmers who had to seek
their bread elsewhere. The start of the grim industrial 'Satanic mills', appalling
conditions and 8 to 12 year old children being used to drag underground carts of
coal etc., well documented in English literature, and permeates the novels of
Charles Dickens

Many sought escape by going to the New World. The Highland Scots, (catholics), and
for their sins in supporting whom they considered to be their rightful  king, (the
catholic James II and his son Bonnie Prince Charlie), after the Battle of Culloden
in 1745 were harshly treated and en masse shipped to the New World. Following the
Irish Potato Famine millions of Irish also emigrated..

Of course you had your own 'industrial' problems in the States. Was it Pennsylvania
(?) - the coal miners - the movie the 'Molly Maguires' starring Richard Harris and
Sean Connery ? And I recall seeing movie shots of labour unrest, at which as a
junior officer, Douglas McArthur was in attendance.

The appalling Industrial Revolution continued its course through the 19th century.
For much of the time it was Liberal politicians who had a conscience and slowly
introduced legislation for social reform. The Conservatives looked another direction
- they were empire builders and had more interest in putting the 'serfs' into the
Queen's scarlet uniform for empire aggrandizement.

First seeds of socialism, modeled on what was going on in the Continent and the
Fabian Society of which the eccentric George Bernard Shaw was a member.  There were
"Labour' members of parliament back in the 1890s ( There's an interesting bio on the
Web about George Lansbury - he was either the actress Angela Lansbury's grandfather
or father, I believe).

Brings us to the 20th century with the above pattern established. The following is a
brief summary from a websitre of which parties held power, the summary doesn't
indicate individual elections  -

1905-1922: Liberal         - Canpbell-Bannerman, Asquith, Coalition - Asquith,  War
                                        then Peace time coalitions - David Lloyd
George
1922-1924: Conservative - Bonar Law, Stanley Baldwin
1924-1924: Labour         - first ever - Ramsey MacDonald
1924-1929  Conservative - Stanley Baldwin
1929-1935 Labour           - Ramsey MacDonald then coalition
1931-1945: Conservative  - Coalition - Baldwin, Neville Chamberlain, Churchill
1945-1951: Labour           - Clement Atlee.
1951-1964 - Conservative - Churchill, Anthony Eden, Harold MacMillan, Alex
                                            Douglas-Home
1964-1970 - Labour         - Harold Wilson
1970-1974 - Conservative  - Edward Heath
1974-1979 - Labour           - Harold Wilson, James Callaghan
1979-1997 - Conservative  - Margaret Thatcher (first female), John Major
1997-200? - Labour           - Tony Blair

What is interesting about the above is the drift, or perhaps more accurately the
'rift' between Left and Right.

Expanding on the above - World War I. Having either volunteered or been conscripted,
the working-class (blue collar workers) were shipped off to France to become cannon
fodder. They returned to Britain horrified by the slaughter and appalled at the
ineptness of their 'leaders' who were from the landed gentry or 'well established in
the City' - Conservatives. It took a while, but in revolt, and sidestepping the
Liberals who were perceived as not too different from the Conservatives, they voted
in Labour administrations. Although still around the Liberals appear to be in a
perpetual Limbo.

The purpose of the above is to show how the political spectrum evolved. Not being a
student of American history, I'd be fascinated to know how it became Democrats and
Republicans and if political thinking is a parallel pattern to the English
Conservatives v Liberals.

Now to personal opportunity. As Donald correctly observed, you can make it in any
modern country. In case you've forgotten, in the past he has also claimed to make 6
figures ! Thirty years ago traveling by rail up to London a dining car waiter told
me there were at least six millionaires who used the route. Currently in England I
can think of Richard Branson, Virgin airlines and records. Recall the name of an
Aussie called Rupert Murdoch ?

Here in Calgary there are at least a dozen millionaires from the oil industry, well
respected and philanthropic. My own brother in '75 was earning SEVEN figures. That's
not from him but his associates, who also note he lost it, and using a English
expression, 'pissed it up against the wall'.

Sure both Judson and Doug can look back at the modest beginnings of their ancestors
and rightly think, "'Boy, I've made it !" Which of course, leads them to the
conclusion that the USA is a great place to live. Which it is. But not for all of
us. I deliberately chose Canada to live, completely discounting the USA. I love your
country to visit, and enjoy meeting with individual people, plus those tourists I
meet up here. But even then I thought your political system was bloody awful - and
that view has solidified now that I'm on this side of the big pond..

Success can occur in any of our democracies. But I still contend - IT IS BEING IN
THE RIGHT PLACE AT THE RIGHT TIME. And either by location, circumstance or
foresight, grabbing the opportunity when it arises. Such an 'opportunity' is still
only for a minority - the rest just remain as hard-working drones.

Jimmy
```

```
James J. Gavan wrote:
> 
> Liam Devlin wrote:
> 
> 
(snip)
>>
>>As far as other countries go, I have no idea, I've lived only in the US.
…[148 more quoted lines elided]…
> only for a minority - the rest just remain as hard-working drones.

Thanks for a good post, Jimmy.

Re: the development of political parties in the US. The oldest party is 
the Democratic Party, which goes back to Adams & Jackson in the 1820's:

   --------------------------------------------------------------
During Adams's administration, new party alignments appeared. Adams's 
followers took the name of "National Republicans," later to be changed 
to "Whigs." Though he governed honestly and efficiently, Adams was not a 
popular president, and his administration was marked with frustrations. 
Adams failed in his effort to institute a national system of roads and 
canals. His years in office appeared to be one long campaign for 
reelection, and his coldly intellectual temperament did not win friends. 
Jackson, by contrast, had enormous popular appeal, especially among his 
followers in the newly named Democratic Party that emerged from the 
Republican Party, with its roots dating back to presidents Jefferson, 
Madison and Monroe. In the election of 1828, Jackson defeated Adams by 
an overwhelming electoral majority.

Jackson -- Tennessee politician, Indian fighter and hero of the Battle 
of New Orleans during the War of 1812 -- drew his support from the small 
farmers of the West, and the workers, artisans and small merchants of 
the East, who sought to use their vote to resist the rising commercial 
and manufacturing interests associated with the Industrial Revolution.

The election of 1828 was a significant benchmark in the trend toward 
broader voter participation. Vermont had universal male suffrage from 
its entry into the Union and Tennessee permitted suffrage for the vast 
majority of taxpayers. New Jersey, Maryland and South Carolina all 
abolished property and tax-paying requirements between 1807 and 1810. 
States entering the Union after 1815 either had universal white male 
suffrage or a low taxpaying requirement. From 1815 to 1821, Connecticut, 
Massachusetts and New York abolished all property requirements. In 1824 
members of the Electoral College were still selected by six state 
legislatures. By 1828 presidential electors were chosen by popular vote 
in every state but Delaware and South Carolina. Nothing dramatized this 
democratic sentiment more than the election of the flamboyant Andrew 
Jackson.

---

WHIGS, DEMOCRATS AND "KNOW-NOTHINGS"

Because Jackson's political opponents had no hope of success so long as 
they remained at cross purposes, they attempted to bring all the 
dissatisfied elements together into a common party called the Whigs. 
Although they organized soon after the election campaign of 1832, it was 
more than a decade before they reconciled their differences and were 
able to draw up a platform. Largely through the magnetism of Henry Clay 
and Daniel Webster, the Whigs' most brilliant statesmen, the party 
solidified its membership. But in the 1836 election, the Whigs were 
still too divided to unite behind a single man or upon a common 
platform. New York's Martin Van Buren, Jackson's vice president, won the 
contest.

An economic depression and the larger-than-life personality of his 
predecessor obscured Van Buren's merits. His public acts aroused no 
enthusiasm, for he lacked the compelling qualities of leadership and the 
dramatic flair that had attended Jackson's every move. The election of 
1840 found the country afflicted with hard times and low wages -- and 
the Democrats on the defensive.

The Whig candidate for president was William Henry Harrison of Ohio, 
vastly popular as a hero of Indian conflicts as well as the War of 1812. 
He was regarded, like Jackson, as a representative of the democratic 
West. His vice presidential candidate was John Tyler -- a Virginian 
whose views on states' rights and a low tariff were popular in the 
South. Harrison won a sweeping victory.

Within a month of his inauguration, however, the 68-year-old Harrison 
died, and Tyler became president. Tyler's beliefs differed sharply from 
those of Clay and Webster, still the most influential men in the 
country. Before Tyler's term was over, these differences led to an open 
break between the president and the party that had elected him.

Americans, however, found themselves divided in more complex ways than 
simple partisan conflicts between Whigs and Democrats. For example, the 
large number of Catholic immigrants in the first half of the 19th 
century, primarily Irish and German, triggered a backlash among 
native-born Protestant Americans.

Immigrants brought more than strange new customs and religious practices 
to American shores. They competed with the native-born for jobs in 
cities along the Eastern seaboard. Moreover, political changes in the 
1820s and 1830s increased the political clout of the foreign born. 
During those two decades, state constitutions were revised to permit 
universal white-male suffrage. This led to the end of rule by patrician 
politicians, who blamed the immigrants for their fall from power. 
Finally, the Catholic Church's failure to support the temperance 
movement gave rise to charges that Rome was trying to subvert the United 
States through alcohol.

The most important of the nativist organizations that sprang up in this 
period was a secret society, the Order of the Star-Spangled Banner, 
founded in 1849. When its members refused to identify themselves, they 
were swiftly labeled the "Know-Nothings." In 1853 the Know-Nothings in 
New York City organized a Grand Council, which devised a new 
constitution to centralize control over the state organizations.

Among the chief aims of the Know-Nothings were an extension in the 
period required for naturalization from five to 21 years, and the 
exclusion of the foreign-born and Catholics from public office. In 1855 
the organization managed to win control of legislatures in New York and 
Massachusetts; by 1855, about 90 U.S. congressmen were linked to the party.

Disagreements over the slavery issue prevented the party from playing a 
role in national politics. The Know-Nothings of the South supported 
slavery while Northern members opposed it. At a convention in 1856 to 
nominate candidates for president and vice president, 42 Northern 
delegates walked out when a motion to support the Missouri Compromise 
was ignored, and the party died as a national force.

---

A DIVIDED NATION

Politically, the 1850s can be characterized as a decade of failure in 
which the nation's leaders were unable to resolve, or even contain, the 
divisive issue of slavery. In 1852, for example, Harriet Beecher Stowe 
published Uncle Tom's Cabin, a novel provoked by the passage of the 
Fugitive Slave Law. When Stowe began writing her book, she thought of it 
as only a minor sketch, but it widened in scope as the work progressed. 
Immediately upon its publication, it caused a sensation. More than 
300,000 copies were sold the first year, and presses ran day and night 
to keep up with the demand.

Although sentimental and full of stereotypes, Uncle Tom's Cabin 
portrayed with undeniable force the cruelty of slavery and the 
fundamental conflict between free and slave societies. The rising 
generation of voters in the North was deeply stirred by the work. It 
inspired widespread enthusiasm for the antislavery cause, appealing as 
it did to basic human emotions -- indignation at injustice and pity for 
the helpless individuals exposed to ruthless exploitation.

In 1854 the old issue of slavery in the territories was renewed and the 
quarrel became more bitter. The region that now comprises Kansas and 
Nebraska was being rapidly settled, increasing pressure for the 
establishment of territorial, and eventually, state governments.

Under terms of the Missouri Compromise of 1820, the entire region was 
closed to slavery. The Compromise of 1850, however, inadvertently 
reopened the question. Dominant slave-holding elements in Missouri, 
objected to letting Kansas become a free territory, for their state 
would then have three free-soil neighbors (Illinois, Iowa and Kansas). 
They feared the prospect of their state being forced to become a free 
state as well. For a time, Missourians in Congress, backed by 
Southerners, blocked all efforts to organize the region.

At this point, Stephen A. Douglas, the Democratic senior senator from 
Illinois, stirred up a storm by proposing a bill, the Kansas-Nebraska 
Act, which enraged all free-soil supporters. Douglas argued that the 
Compromise of 1850, which left Utah and New Mexico free to resolve the 
slavery issue for themselves, superseded the Missouri Compromise. His 
plan called for two territories, Kansas and Nebraska, and permitted 
settlers to carry slaves into them. The inhabitants themselves were to 
determine whether they should enter the Union as free or slave states.

Northerners accused Douglas of currying favor with the South in order to 
gain the presidency in 1856. Angry debates marked the progress of the 
bill. The free-soil press violently denounced it. Northern clergymen 
assailed it. Businessmen who had hitherto befriended the South suddenly 
turned about-face. Yet in May 1854, the Kansas-Nebraska Act passed the 
Senate amid the boom of cannon fired by Southern enthusiasts. When 
Douglas subsequently visited Chicago to speak in his own defense, the 
ships in the harbor lowered their flags to half-mast, the church bells 
tolled for an hour and a crowd of 10,000 hooted so loudly that he could 
not make himself heard.

The immediate results of Douglas's ill-starred measure were momentous. 
The Whig Party, which had straddled the question of slavery expansion, 
sank to its death, and in its stead a powerful new organization arose, 
the Republican Party, whose primary demand was that slavery be excluded 
from all the territories. In 1856, it nominated John Fremont, whose 
expeditions into the Far West had won him renown. Although Fremont lost 
the election, the new Republican Party swept a great part of the North. 
Such free-soil leaders as Salmon P. Chase and William Seward exerted 
greater influence than ever. Along with them appeared a tall, lanky 
Illinois attorney, Abraham Lincoln.

The flow of both Southern slave holders and antislavery families into 
Kansas resulted in armed conflict, and soon the territory was being 
called "bleeding Kansas." Other events brought the nation still closer 
to upheaval: notably, the Supreme Court's infamous 1857 decision 
concerning Dred Scott.

Scott was a Missouri slave who, some 20 years earlier, had been taken by 
his master to live in Illinois and the Wisconsin Territory, where 
slavery had been banned by the Northwest Ordinance. Returning to 
Missouri and becoming discontented with his life there, Scott sued for 
liberation on the ground of his residence on free soil. The Supreme 
Court -- dominated by Southerners -- decided that Scott lacked standing 
in court because he was not a citizen; that the laws of a free state 
(Illinois) had no effect on his status because he was the resident of a 
slave state (Missouri); and that slave holders had the right to take 
their "property" anywhere in the federal territories and that Congress 
could not restrict the expansion of slavery. The Court's decision thus 
invalidated the whole set of comprise measures by which Congress for a 
generation had tried to settle the slavery issue.

The Dred Scott decision stirred fierce resentment throughout the North. 
Never before had the Court been so bitterly condemned. For Southern 
Democrats, the decision was a great victory, since it gave judicial 
sanction to their justification of slavery throughout the territories.


--------------------------------------------------------

A little sidebar on the treatment of the American Indians:

WESTWARD

...

Overall, the growth of the nation was enormous: population grew from 
7.25 million to more than 23 million from 1812 to 1852, and the land 
available for settlement increased by almost the size of Europe -- from 
4.4 million to 7.8 million square kilometers. Still unresolved, however, 
were the basic conflicts rooted in sectional differences which, by the 
decade of the 1860s, would explode into civil war. Inevitably, too, this 
westward expansion brought settlers into conflict with the original 
inhabitants of the land: the Indians.

In the first part of the 19th century, the most prominent figure 
associated with these conflicts was Andrew Jackson, the first 
"Westerner" to occupy the White House. In the midst of the War of 1812, 
Jackson, then in charge of the Tennessee militia, was sent into southern 
Alabama, where he ruthlessly put down an uprising of Creek Indians. The 
Creeks soon ceded two-thirds of their land to the United States. Jackson 
later routed bands of Seminole Indians from their sanctuaries in 
Spanish-owned Florida.

In the 1820s, President Monroe's secretary of war, John C. Calhoun, 
pursued a policy of removing the remaining tribes from the old Southwest 
and resettling them beyond the Mississippi. Jackson continued this 
policy as president.

In 1830 Congress passed the Indian Removal Act, providing funds to 
transport the eastern tribes beyond the Mississippi. In 1834 a special 
Indian territory was set up in what is now Oklahoma. In all, the tribes 
signed 94 treaties during Jackson's two terms, ceding millions of 
hectares to the federal government and removing dozens of tribes from 
their ancestral homelands.

Perhaps the most egregious chapter in this unfortunate history concerned 
the Cherokees, whose lands in western North Carolina and Georgia had 
been guaranteed by treaty since 1791. Among the most progressive of the 
eastern tribes, the Cherokees' fate was sealed when gold was discovered 
on their land in 1829. Even a favorable ruling from the Supreme Court 
proved little help. With the acquiescence of the Jackson administration, 
the Cherokees were forced to make the long and cruel trek to Oklahoma in 
1835. Many died of disease and privation in what became known as the 
"Trail of Tears."
   --------------------------------------------------------------

All from: http://usinfo.state.gov/usa/infousa/facts/history/toc.htm

ideologies & allegiances.

Not surprisingly. I think most people assume that Ireland didn't produce 
any food during the potato famine in the mid 19th century. Not true, the 
country continued to produce a bounty of everything except potatoes and

starving tenant farmers. The English Parliament, along with the land 
owners, decided that "market forces" should decide how the food should 
be allocated. Everyone was free to purchase as much as he could afford. 
I remain extremely sceptical of the "magic" of market forces to insure 
that the markets are efficient, viz. recent excesses of capitalism in 
the US.

military for a few bombs dropped in the sea.  There was a lot of
American blood spilled getting the Germans out of your country.  If you
Europeans were as eager to show some gratitude as to criticize, we
would pay more attention to what you have to say.  We virtually never

"Gabriele Boccone" <gabriele.osi@libero.it> wrote:
> >> My 2 cents from Italy:
> >>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> after that war.

Wrong.

> That means:
> - We used that kind of bomb on Japan, and that kind of chemical weapon
>    in Vietnam, because we are good.

Wrong.  We used it to remove the foliage, not as a 'chemical weapon',
you ignoramus.  We did it because the NVA was using the jungle as
cover, and lives are more important than vegetation, and also because
the long-term effects of  the chemicals wasn't known then. It's one thing
to make a mistake, it's quite another thing to intentionally commit an
evil act.  Even good people with good intentions can make a mistake.
And it's a third thing to be an ignoramus and intentionally distort facts
to fit your own personal bias.

> - Nobody should use that kind of weapons again, because they are bad.

If anyone has passed any kind of restrictions on using defoliants to
remove foliage, either in wartime or peace, I have never heard of it.

Do you equate the use of a firearm by a policeman to shoot someone
who is trying to murder a little child, with a criminal using a firearm
to shoot am innocent robbery victim?  If you do, then you are *way*
too simple. It is the act that creates the results, but it is the intention
that must be judged. Only fools look simply at an act without regard
for cause or intention.

People such as yourself who have an 'anti U.S.' mentality like to
conveniently forget that the use of the atomic bomb in WWI actually
*saved* literally millions of *Japanese* lives. I have seen actual
video footage, taken in the 70's (see "The World at War", a 26 part
documentary of WWII by the BBC), of high officials in the Japanese
government in 1945 (including the man who signed the surrender
agreement on the USS Missouri), and they said that if we had not
dropped the atom bombs, it would have been impossible for them to
have surrendered, and it was nearly so as it was. Many Japanese
fought on, even after the surrender. Had the U.S. been forced to
invade Japan, literally millions of innocent civilians would have
been inevitably killed.  The same documentary also shows young
Japanese elementary school children being taught to use knives and
sharp sticks to fight if the U.S. did invade. You also like to forget
that on one night a bombing raid of 2,000 B29's struck Tokyo,
burning the city to ground and killing over 170,000, more than the
150,000 who were killed by *both* atom bombs combined. (You
do that because it is Politically Correct to imagine there is a moral
difference between killing 100,000 people with conventional
weapons and killing 100,000 people with nuclear weapons.) It is
not *how* you kill people, but *why* you kill people that is the
moral difference. The how is a practical, not a moral, difference.

You also like to forget that at the time, Japan was occupying vast
areas of Asia and Indonesia, keeping the populations down with
such brutality that even the Nazis complained (e.g. the slaughter in
broad daylight of 135,000 innocent Manchurian civilians in one
day and the Bataan Death March). Even with nearly 60 years of
hindsight, the U.S. had only three real options at the time:
1. Cease hostilities against Japan, with no surrender, leaving
Japan to keep the territory they had already conquered and, except
for England and China who were also at war with Japan, free to
rebuild, reinforce and take more territory, also leaving the ones in
power who started the Pacific war (in 1933, not 1941) in the first
place.
2. Invade Japan conventionally. In early 1945 plans were already
on the table for this option. The estimates were that there would
be at least one million American casualties. Based on figures
from earlier campaigns with the Japanese, their casualties would
have been several million, most of them fatalities. Many people
today do not realize what an implacable foe the Japanese were,
often refusing to surrender, fighting literally until the last man.
Even after the official surrender, some Japanese units refused to
surrender, and had to be completely destroyed. Japanese civilians
were brainwashed to believe they would be tortured and killed by
victorious Americans, and many of them committed suicide, some
by jumping of cliffs while holding their children, before the
Americans got to them.  The Americans were horrified by this.
3. Use the atomic bomb to destroy two Japanese cities, killing
150,000 Japanese and no Americans.

Check your history, you will discover that all of the above is pure
fact. Now genius, which option is the more moral?

out of Kuwait. In every case, Americans were fighting and dying defending
the freedom of others. Only an ignoramus would be glad for U.S. help in
defending *their* homeland against aggression, and then criticize the U.S.
for doing the same thing for others. A hypocritical ignoramus.

would think those demonstrations of support for Sadam are real.  Those
propel would be shot if they expressed anything less than absolute fidelity
to Sadam, who has executed a number of his own family members who

> >> to fly very low (and sometimes, kill people by "accidentally" cutting
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> nobody will ever prosecute them.

And other pilots will *continue* to fly like that, because it is people like
that who make the best fighter pilots. People who are less aggressive and
more willing to 'play by the rules' generally get their butts shot out of the
air by those who are more aggressive and less willing to 'play by the rules'.
Like it or not (you obviously don't know it), that's just the way it works.

> And I think that *at least* they should be sent to jail. They were doing
> something highly DANGEROUS and ILLEGAL, and they were released,
> as if they were innocent.

It was a very tragic accident, involving serious loss of innocent life. But
in reality, like I said above, if you want the most effective fighter pilots
you can get (and U.S. fighter pilots are the best, as has been demonstrated
many times), having pilots who are 'cowboys' that 'push the envelope' is
simply something you have to live with. You don't have to like it, but you
do have to live with it, or have incompetent fighter pilots. In peacetime,
incidents like that are hard to stomach. But when the Enemy is knocking
on your door, you had better hope that you have the most aggressive,
pedal-to-the-metal pilots in the sky. Reality is not always pleasant, but
it is always reality.

> >> Of course terrorists are bad, but you simply can't kill 300 innocent
> >> people to get 1 terrorist. You are putting it like it was *too
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> you can use a hammer, why use dynamite?

A military isn't like a hammer or a screwdriver, or even dynamite.  You
can go down to the hardware store and buy those any time you need them,
or you can buy them ahead of time and keep them in a drawer or garage,
without having to maintain them. When you need a military, you can't just
go down to the store and buy one. You have to spend lots of money and
it takes a long time to build one. And you have to spend a lot of money
and time maintaining a military, or it will be useless when needed. It is
fashionable in the world today to think a strong military isn't needed.
But there has never been a time in history when countries neglected their
defense without paying a price, eventually. Human nature hasn't changed
in recorded history. Sadam is virtually a clone of Stalin. Both cold and
heartless and absolutely ruthless. And there were people like them 5,000
years ago. As long as people like that continue to exist and have power,
a wise country keeps a well maintained military. A wealthy and wise
country keeps a military that can kick anybody's butt any time. In the long
run, it is the cheapest and easiest thing to do. No sane enemy attacks
someone who can clean their clock without breaking a sweat. But you
need to let them know you can do that, or your fine military is not a
deterrent at all. Then you have to really use it to clean their clock when
the time comes, and war is not fun, even when you win. Deterrence is
much better, which is why the U.S. makes it known what we can do, and
sends a carrier group around when trouble is likely. Not for braggadocio,
but for deterrence. Better to intimidate than to clobber. But better to do
either than to fail to defend.

> And the last thing: you are probably 100% European blood, and the
> only people with a right to say "I am American" are the native
> American (indians).

At what point can you call yourself a 'native'? If you examine history,
you will find that most every place has been occupied from time to
time by various peoples. Once a culture is established and stable, then
the citizens of that culture are 'natives'. Any other interpretation does
not fit with the reality of history.

> Your family name tells your history. Your history, since probably
> 1700 or 1800, was in Scotland, then you "stopped" being European
> and begun being American.

Actually, Scottish and Welch.  I can trace my ancestry back to Welch
royalty. There's also a bit of American Indian in there too, for parts
of my family have been in America for a long time.

> Most American people tend to ignore history, since *their* history
> starts in 1700-1800.

Pardon. American recorded history (the Indians recorded no written
history) begins for earnest in the 1500's. In this particular discussion,
it is clearly apparent that at least one Italian is ignoring history.

>And their names are mostly English, Spanish, Italian, and even Chinese.
> Then: you don't know when, or how those wars started, and you think
> you can drop some bomb and have people stop fighting.

Sorry, your point (if any) escapes me. Odd that you mention ignorance
of history and say that (to paraphrase) making war on people won't stop
them from fighting. Actually, our experience in America has been that
annihilating someone's ability to threaten you is quite effective in
stopping them fighting. It sure worked in Japan and Europe, and
(unfortunately) with the American Indians. Only an ignoramus, entirely
ignorant of history, would claim that violence never solves anything.
No criminal, once executed, committed another crime.  And no country
whose entire military infrastructure had been destroyed, continued a
war. Often 'violence begets violence'. But sometimes the right kind of
violence results in peace. Just as the U.S. has been friends at peace
with Italy, Germany and Japan since WWII.

```
On Fri, 30 Aug 2002 09:18:03 GMT, "Gabriele Boccone"
<gabriele.osi@libero.it> enlightened us:

>>> That means:
>>> - We used that kind of bomb on Japan, and that kind of chemical
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I can't comment on the atom bomb because the U.S. dropped it on the
two Japanese islands knowing its effects and wanting to send a signal
to Japan that continued war making would result in this type of
devastation to the entire country.  While a terrible thing, I believe
it saved many more lives than it actually took.  But our Italian
friend is pretty much right on about the use of Agent Orange and
Judson is maybe not aware of the facts.  

Agent Orange -- so called because it was shipped to
Vietnam in 55-gallon drums marked with an orange stripe
-- was the principal defoliant used in what the Pentagon
called Operation Ranch Hand. It was a potent witches'
brew that contained equal measures of two powerful
chemicals, whose effectiveness had been tested at the
Pentagon's War Research Service at Fort Detrick,
Maryland, and field-tested first in the Florida Everglades
and Puerto Rico, and later in Thailand and Vietnam.
Though scientists, including 17 Nobel laureates, and
organizations like the Federation of American Scientists
and the American Association for the Advancement of
Science had been calling for a halt to Operation Ranch
Hand for years during the Vietnam conflict -- branding it
"barbarous" and a dangerous precedent for biological and
chemical warfare -- the U.S. military repeatedly
dismissed concerns about possible health and
environmental effects. Then, in 1969, scientists
discovered that one of the components of Agent
Orange, known by the shorthand notation 2,4,5-T,
caused birth defects in laboratory animals. In December
1970, President Richard Nixon announced a halt to the
use of Agent Orange in Vietnam. 

Further research showed the dioxin in Agent Orange led to a wide range
of diseases including birth defects and cancers.

But it was not only the Vietnamese people who suffered from Agent
Orange's use.  Many, many soldiers who were exposed to it have had
health problems and it took the Veterans Administration years before
the Pentagon would finally admit the diseases suffered by these
soldiers was caused by Agent Orange.

Just recently the U.S. held meetings with Vietnam and has agreed to
help them treat their citizens and lands that have been effected by
Agent Orange.


<<much snippage>>


((some information taken from a much longer and interesting article on
this issue at:
http://www.motherjones.com/mother_jones/JF00/orange.html  ))

higher.

blame.  Others were far more culpable but have yet to be brought to

This comment from Judson, I do need to respond to.  He said,

 "You think our objection to Joseph Stalin, who shot and starved millions of
his own people, was a  *religious issue*? You can look at the social,
economic and ecological  wasteland that the USSR made of western Europe, and
say that our  objections were a *religious issue*?"

I don't think that it was "religious" in the sense of OUR *religion* versus
theirs.  But I certainly do believe that the US position toward the USSR was
treated with "religious furor" and not rational.  Remember most (certainly
NOT all) of the starving of Stalin's own people occurred BEFORE WWII when we
(the US citizens) were told what a GREAT guy he was.

We started treating the USSR as an "evil empire" (later term) about the time
that our "good friend" England was trying (and failing) to do to India,
exactly what USSR was trying (and succeeding) to do to much of Eastern
Europe.  As far as I can tell, the only REAL difference was that the US
"thought" that we would have equal influence in Eastern Europe after WWII -
while we "knew" that it was England that would have the influence in India.

"William M. Klein" wrote:

> It is EXACTLY the 1945 - 1947 period of time that I was talking about.  It
> was during this time that the US started to "hate" the USSR (and "good old
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…

Well back to the Russkies - In defence of the US - there's a turn-up for the
book <G>.

I'm not familiar with the period, but I really can't believe that the US
population was being fed that Josef was a 'good guy' back in the Thirties. Like
the UK, US intelligence must have been blatantly aware of the horror side of
Communism.

Churchill had first hand knowledge when he was Home Secretary back in 1910-ish.
There was the 'Sydney Street incident' involving Marxists, (the mystery around
it suggests one of them was Lenin). Following on from the Communists early moves
in 1917, the British supported failed attempts by the White Russians. I'm sure
the US must have been cognizant of these events.

Communism even in its mildest idealistic form was seen as a threat to stability
in many European states. I've no idea but you probably also had, for a short
time, a US Communist Party. Logic would suggest so, as many US-Germans, with
Lindbergh in particular, were sympathetic to the Nazis, primarily because
something was being done for the Fatherland. We know Communism was a threat in
Germany, and we know what Hitler did about it.

My own conclusion - both US and UK were anti-Communist through the Thirties,
solidified by the Nazi-Soviet. pact that led to the carving up of Poland when
attacked in 1939. Britain entered WW2, still with that view - I don't know the
history - but I'm willing to bet the same attitude existed in the States.

For public consumption Hollywood produced one of Greta Garbo's last movies,
"Ninotchka" where she played an attractive but bureaucratic commissar. So Josef
and his gang were seen as a joke. Then Hitler, ignoring Napoleon's career,
decided to attack the Russians. Don't ask me the name of the movie - totally
forgotten. We now swing 360 degrees - the poor Soviets are our buddies. Walter
Huston playing the sage Russian doctor, and just to get the atmosphere right,
Erich von Stronheim, complete with shaven head, playing the Nazi Prussian
monster.

Then our good old buddy Winston Churchill gets rebuked for supporting the
Russkies. Can't remember exact quote, so a paraphrase, "I would give the Devil a
letter of reference if he would support us in getting rid of the Nazi terror..."

Were the Americans and British sheer hypocrites in supporting the Soviets. A
RESOUNDING NO. Expediency dictated that they should be used as a resource to
take on the Nazis and they bore the brunt of the battles until D-Day 1944. We
know only too well that the Soviets were spying on us, just as the OSS/CIA and
MI5/MI6 were spying on them. Back to the movies, a black and white, "House on
92nd Street".

We knew at the Yalta Conference that Josef was a double dealing bastard, and
poor old FDR was already too ill to be effective. Harry Truman took over, but
the
European game plan had already been set - the biggest mistake - the US standing
back and letting the Commies take Berlin.

We know the Soviets were constantly testing us - firstly Churchill coining the
"Iron Curtain", then Kruschev's 'Missles of October' to test the mettle of the
Irish-Americn President, followed by his "We will bury you", leading eventually
to the non-combatant movie star and his 'Star Wars" approach which was a
counter, "We will bury you with greenbacks".

I don't think western powers have anything to feel guilty about - they were
responding to real politick. If one does want to point the finger, yes the US
was guilty of allowing an opportunistic slime ball like McCarthy, to institute
his witch hunt.

The thing that always amazes me, considering the amount of money poured into the
intelligence agencies - when Mikhail Gorbachev announced the demise of
Communism, I think both the CIA and MI5 were just as surprised as I was ! I
don't once recall seeing a talking head on TV saying, "I worked for the CIA and
back in 19.. we knew Communism was gong down the tubes.............".

Jimmy

>

Judson McClendon wrote:

> "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:
> >
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> politicians running off at the mouth. The KGB was very, very good.

Can't address the US situation but there was a very good reason for infiltration in
the UK - homosexuality. In an age when we were less enlightened - now we are more
understanding and say "It's not my inclination but I sympathize with you". Certainly
the Oxford/Cambridge set up during the 1930s was full of idealists, many veering
towards socialism and even a mild interest in communism. Additionally, a
considerable number were homosexuals. A French female politician referred to it as
le malade Anglais (the English disease).

This was a good territory for the KGB to blackmail people into espionage . Some
didn't have to be blackmailed, such as Guy Burgess, Donald Maclean and Kim Philby
who were senior MI5/6 officers - having defected it was discovered that they were
also responsible for leaks of CIA information - their data without doubt,
contributed to the death of agents on the ground..

More close to home - I trained as a 17-year old with somebody in the RAF. We were
into amateur dramatics, I finished up in make-up trying to look fortyish while he
would be playing the female lead. After training I joined the staff of the Inspector
General of the RAF and he became the 'secretary' to the C-in-C, Fighter Command.
He was at Stanmore and myself Richmond and then Hendon, so both being on the
perimeter of London, perhaps once in six months we would meet in central London. (OK
- Downtown London to you lot <G>).

He was 'mad' about the theatre and spent much of his time in central London in
theatrical circles. The KGB was watching. I wasn't aware of his homosexuality, (Boy
! Were we a a naive lot back in my generation !). They blackmailed him - get the
Battle Order of Fighter Command from his boss's safe. That easy. Suddenly there ws
my semi-friiend's face splashed all across the British dailies. A court martial gave
him a 12 year sentence.

PS: I've always speculated - did that association ever screw my own chances of
getting a commission ???

Jimmy
```

Most of this stuff I ad lib from memory, but went back to my kiddy encyclopedia
to confirm the reason for this one. Technically this was not 'Empire building'.
The Russians were putting the squeeze on the Turkish empire to protect orthodox
christians. The British with the phobia of the Russian Bear gaining access to
India via Afghanistan via the Kyhber Pass, or to the Middle East via Turkey,
along with the French and Sardinia, (I wonder what the hell the last
contributed !), supported the Turks and started the Crimean War, 1854-56 -
giving us Florence Nightingale and the fiasco of the Charge of the Light
Brigade, (Tennyson - "Half a league, Half a league, Half a league
onwards....").

Remember my granddaddy at Omdurman, Egypt, (or Sudan ?) in 1898 ? That was the
last 'Empire' cavalry charge which Winston Churchill participated in as a
junior officer.

Jimmy

great majority of the airpower.  Since the EU is more populous then the
U.S., and has a larger economy than the U.S., and Bosnia is in Europe
after all, doesn't that sound a bit lopsided to you?  It does to me.

> Bosnia was a _NATO_ action, they acted in unison and it was _not_ the
> US that 'stepped' in, it was the whole of NATO in which the US is a part.

As I said, I understand it was a cooperative process.  But had it not
been for U.S. political pressure to deal with the situation, and our
willingness to take the majority of the load, I am very doubtful that
anyone in Europe would have intervened, unless the conflict spilled
over the borders.  The Europeans should have dealt with the situation
in their own backyard, whether the U.S. did anything or not.  I think
most Americans didn't want the U.S. to get involved in Bosnia at all.
Historically, if Japan had not attacked Pearl Harbor, it is very likely
that the U.S. would not have gone to war.  But it is also likely that
Germany would have eventually either declared war on the U.S., or
provoked us into declaring war on them, because we were deeply
involved in helping England (e.g. Lend-Lease and our using U.S.
warships to guard convoys going from here to England).  Actually,
you could say that Japan 'did us a favor' in a backhanded sort of way,
by dragging us into the war.

> The Americans expect their press to report how the 'US Calavary'
> charged in and 'saved the day', so they do - bugger the facts.

I certainly won't defend the American press, except to say that a very
few sources (e.g. Fox News) do try to show all sides of issues.  One
of my pet gripes is the bias and incompetence of most of the American
media.

as a result of fighting germany, but also to prevent american
anti-communist war-mongering. In fact just like America's move into
western europe was alledgedly to 'prevent' the USSR's anti-capitalist
war-mongering.

At the end of WWII America had the atom bomb, the military strength, and
an anti-communist attitude sufficient to continue the war against Russia
at the slightest provocation.  Hence the cold war while Russia
manouvered into a position where it was equal to America and thus war
was less likely.

Cuba, for example, was entirely to balance America's weapons on Russia's
door step with some on America's.
```

```
"Alistair Maclean" <ld50macca@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
>
> Judson McClendon <jud@sunvaley0.com> writes
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> military conquest since the 1500s?

Most of the 'contiguous 48 states'.  Alaska was purchased from Russia
(for about $9 million, IIRC), Long Island ('purchased' from the Indians
for trinkets) and the 'Louisiana Purchase' being some exceptions.

The 'conquest' of what is now the U.S. was a very different process than
what happened with Russia.  In the case of Russia, there were defined
neighboring countries that Russia decided to make war on to conquer for
the purposes of annexation.  In the case of the U.S., it was (generally)
the case of people gradually moving into areas not defined as countries,
and when conflict arose, fighting to keep the land the people already
occupied.  It was not all that clear cut, of course.  In the cases where the
U.S. went to war to obtain territory, it was (except for the Indians, who
fit under the above) the conquest of a 'possession', not an established
sovereign nation.

The one *really big* difference is that no state in the U.S. would choose
to be independent (except the Indian Territories which aren't technically
part of the 'states').  Even in the American Civil War, it wasn't really a
case of the southern states generally wanting to be independent, it was a
virtual single issue dispute that motivated the southern states to secede.
Not so of Russia.  There has never been a time when all of the parts of
Russia wanted to be Russian, and none of the east European countries
annexed by Russia after WWII wanted to be.  Chechnya being a recent
example of the former, and the rest of east Europe being recent examples
(actually, the whole set) of the latter.
```

I did not intend to give the impression that I was excusing it.  I have
made several clear statements in these threads that I definitely do not
justify what was done.  But surely you have to admit that settlers
encroaching on territory loosely inhabited by nomadic tribes is not the
same as deliberately crossing a formally established border.  Not that
all the Indian tribes were nomadic, of course.  And surely you would
admit that if Canada were to annex Greenland it would not be quite the
same thing as Canada invading Denmark, just as stealing your auto is
not the same as kidnapping you.  Not that either would be okay.  But I
(and probably anybody else) would rather have a neighbor who might
steal your garden hose or lawnmower than a neighbor who might kidnap
your children or yourself.  There are degrees of right and wrong, and
that was the point I was trying to make.
