```
Judson McClendon wrote:
> 
> But surely you have to admit that settlers
> encroaching on territory loosely inhabited by nomadic tribes is not the
> same as deliberately crossing a formally established border.

Stealing is stealing.

It may not be the same to _you_ but it is the same to those invaded.
Eventually the 'encroachment' takes _all_, or all the useful,
territory.  

One may also claim that killing off a few settlement farmers who had
enroached too far is 'not the same' as attacking a town or a fort.

In fact your claim is _EXACTLY_ the problem that is causing the
terrorism that resulted in 9/11.  The Isrealies are 'encroaching on
territory loosely inhabited' by Palestinians.  In fact they have lived
there for several generations.  Israel (backed by US) claims 'god gave
us this and dominion over all non-people, such as native inhabitants'
and thinks that they can just move settlers in.

The displaced natives fight back and are termed terrorists and
suppressed.  Others in support make somewhat more effective attacks such
as 9/11.

Americans didn't 'get it' with the Indians, and they obviously still
don't 'get it'.

>  And surely you would
> admit that if Canada were to annex Greenland it would not be quite the
> same thing as Canada invading Denmark,

Did you think that Argentina 'annexing' the Falkland Islands was OK, or
China 'annexing' Tibet, or Iraq 'annexing' Kuwait ?  Actually in the
last case Kuwait used to be part of Iraq so it was 'recovering' its
soverign territory.

> just as stealing your auto is
> not the same as kidnapping you. 

People live in Greenland.  To them it is kidnapping.  To the Indians is
was kidnapping, to the Palestinians is is kidnapping.

> Not that either would be okay.  But I
> (and probably anybody else) would rather have a neighbor who might
> steal your garden hose or lawnmower than a neighbor who might kidnap
> your children or yourself.  There are degrees of right and wrong, and
> that was the point I was trying to make.

And what if they just moved into your house from down the road and told
you to bugger off or they would shoot you ?  And what if this happened
several times after you had built new houses. Would you still be saying
how nice those people who now lived in your houses were ?
```

```
"Richard Plinston" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:3D759E65.2278934C@Azonic.co.nz...
> Judson McClendon wrote:
> >
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>

One of the real problems for the native peoples of North America is that
land is not considered "ownable".  That the actual land is part of the
people's heritage, and cannot be owned by any individual is, to me, a
culturally superior belief system.  The european tradition was that the land
was owned by a single person, the king, and most of the subsequent law
results from individuals attempting to get their share back.

To a very large extent, our current woes with the atmosphere are a direct
result of that idea ... that somehow each little chunk of the world is
"owned" by one specific individual, and that individual has a right to do
any damn thing they please, provided they stay inside the boundaries of
"their" property.

That one dwells on this earth as a guest of the earth, and that each of us
is steward for the plot we live on, and responsible for keeping it safe and
sound for our people, is not only closer to the truth, to my mind, but a
spiritually more solid basis for an ethical existance.

Yet, Judson, in the above paragraph, you are using that very idea as a
weapon to justify making the natives leave, so that whites could take the
land over.  While I can see it being justified by european law, I cannot see
it working for the forseeable future. Comments?

Donald
```

```

On  4-Sep-2002, Liam Devlin <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net> wrote:

> >>Unless the alternative is to give that power to the king.   We all know of
> >>"kings" who are not worthy of such trusts, or who don't have the detailed
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> ownership is purely European.

Not purely.

As long as the land is not densely used, this works.   When power comes from
controlling valuable land, the king will grab that power.  One way of limiting
this grab is to give certain powers and rights to the individuals who are using
that land.  Densely populated places that have moved away from dictator powers
have done so by establishing property rights for land, buildings, and
businesses.  It isn't absolute - but the state has to do more work to force you
off you home.

It has other effects - we won't go into debt for 20-30 years to build a house if
we don't see that we have rights of ownership of that house.  We sometimes give
up ownership of the minerals under the ground, but we want to believe we will
get our investment back.   Aborigines didn't require this security to get a
return on their house building investment.
```

```
On  5-Sep-2002, Donald Tees <Donald_Tees@sympatico.ca> wrote:

> Some places have.  Others have done so by making making lands communal.
> The United States seems to operate on the principal that money is the
> only factor that works, and that the richest automatically have rights
> to do anything.

Not so much with land.  The whole concept with the book/movie Shane was that the
rich rancher had rights to lots of public land.  The small farmers got the
rights and by owning the land, his way of life was doomed.

Land in amounts to make the rich powerful is very, very expensive.  Sure Turner
has his buffalo ranch, but that is more of a toy than a way to acquire
money/power.  There is less and less reason for the rich to own vast amounts of
land all the time - and they don't.

But government land can still be abused, when people instead choose to lobby
non-owing politicians for the right to use "public" land.

Our entire western world seems to be of the opinion
> that poisening the water and air are perfectly acceptable if there is
> money to be made at it.  If we do not change that, we are all going to
> die, pure and simple.

The pollutants you are referring to have been lessening tremendously in rich
countries.  Developing countries produce far more poisons, and poor countries do
more ecological damage with slash and burning.  (by forcing Nike to shut down
its plants, their hope of making it out of the slash and burn ecology declines).

But the biggest pollutants by far (with the possible exception of mining the
oceans - which nobody "owns"), are buildings, roads, and farms.   Try to grow
something on a highway.  Measure the temperature above a plowed field.    These
aren't addressed in any accord because the world isn't about to give up the
wealth/power of population.  Even if it kills us.
```

```
Did they have individual homes as we think of them? Many American

investment". AFAIK, that's a European concept, too.

native peoples by the Europeans. I wonder if anyone has set up a 
simulation to see what the US or Australia might be like now if the 
Europeans hadn't come?
```

```
"Liam Devlin" <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net> wrote:
>
> IIRC the aborigines in Australia had/have the same belief. The notion
> of ownership is purely European.

Actually, it would be more accurate to say primitive cultures have not
had the need to develop the concepts of ownership of real property,
simply because they are primitive cultures.  No advanced society
could function under such a philosophy, because you must be able to
depend on stability of occupation to develop more than very crude
technology.  Even a mill to grind grain using water power requires
permanent occupation of the location.  Such things are developed by
individuals looking to better themselves, not by nomadic groups.
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> 
> On  3-Sep-2002, "Donald Tees" <donald_tees@sympatico.ca> wrote:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> the more important such safeguards are.

No. Rather, such safeguards need to be introduced along with other 
institutions, if those institutions are not to end up unbalanced. But before 
those institutions are introduced, you can get by without the safeguards - as 
long as you are also willing to get by without whatever positive things the 
institutions deliver.

That's all very abstract. But try this: in Europe, all those levels of 
society - EVEN commoners - got their particular privileges within a feudal 
framework. And in North America, people felt no need of special defences 
against central power in the days when that was an authority in name only; 
the American War of Independence was fought in defence of old practices, not 
of old principles. The principles were actually being trampled on by the 
rebels, only to be resurrected in a new form that was to flog them with 
scorpions. PML.
```

```
"Donald Tees" <donald_tees@sympatico.ca> wrote:
>
> That the actual land is part of the people's heritage, and cannot be
> owned by any individual is, to me, a culturally superior belief system.

When the fellow down the street moves into your home, without your
permission, on that very basis, let us know how you enjoy it.  ;-)
Individual ownership of land is one of the most important ways to
make people free there is.

> The european tradition was that the land
> was owned by a single person, the king, and most of the subsequent law
> results from individuals attempting to get their share back.

The European system was no better, probably worse.  It is the
individual ownership of real property that is liberating.  This is why
it is one of the fundamental principles the U.S. was founded on.

> To a very large extent, our current woes with the atmosphere are a direct
> result of that idea ... that somehow each little chunk of the world is
> "owned" by one specific individual, and that individual has a right to do
> any damn thing they please, provided they stay inside the boundaries of
> "their" property.

That statement is not correct.  Ownership od a piece of land does not
give you the right to dump on the fellow next door.  It not the ownership
of land that is the problem, but callous disreguard to propriety and the
rights of others.

> That one dwells on this earth as a guest of the earth, and that each of us
> is steward for the plot we live on, and responsible for keeping it safe and
> sound for our people, is not only closer to the truth, to my mind, but a
> spiritually more solid basis for an ethical existance.

Except for the 'guest of the earth' part, I agree with the above. It is not a
'guest' who exercises stweardship, but one in authority, such as owner or
overseer.  A guest has no right to appropriate anything without permission
from the owner.  Historically, the idea that the earth is some sort of entity
drrives from the Gaia philosophy created by those who have practiced
witchraft for thousands of years.

> Yet, Judson, in the above paragraph, you are using that very idea as a
> weapon to justify making the natives leave, so that whites could take the
> land over.  While I can see it being justified by european law, I cannot
> see it working for the forseeable future. Comments?

Where in the world do you get the idea that I think the Europeans had
a right to "make the natives leave?"  Use the land along with the
Indians, yes.  Run them all off and kill them, no.  What I *am* trying to
say is this idea that the settlers came over here with the specific idea
of running the Indians off and killing them is a bunch of bunk.  That
was a situation that developed over time, in which the Indians and the
settlers both had blame.

You and I both have the same right to use a public park.  Neither of us
has the right to exclude the other.  This applied to the Indians, because
of their own view of the land not being owned by any individual.  This
does not apply to your property that you personally own.  Either the land
was for all to use, or it was owned by individuals.  If the land was for
all, not owned by anyone, then the Indians had no basis to exclude the
settlers.  The Indian philosophy about the land only works for small,
nomadic communities.  For any (technologically) more advanced culture
it is tantamount to anarchy.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT] Gaia (was: Re: Presidential eligibility)

_(reply depth: 38)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2002-09-10T10:33:21-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<allad1$f1i$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<ak4c8s$1f0$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <giTa9.79375$%v4.3291143@e3500-atl2.usenetserver.com> <RD3b9.4021$if4.955838@news20.bellglobal.com> <WI4b9.32347$Hd4.7298397@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com> <217e491a.0208281309.79f8a8ca@posting.google.com> <TFbb9.35054$Hd4.7593386@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com> <217e491a.0208301358.6107e05a@posting.google.com> <_c4c9.9068$FJ1.572354@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com> <3D71D6BB.A300CFD1@Azonic.co.nz> <G0dc9.6784$2E6.2863081@newssvr28.news.prodigy.com> <217e491a.0208312221.3f6eb9af@posting.google.com> <IVpc9.20800$FJ1.3067903@e3500-atl1.usenetserver.com> <sihyyTAMxmc9EwqB@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> <a4Lc9.45881$vY2.1109453@e3500-atl2.usenetserver.com> <s9jiqLA7q6c9EwLc@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> <DFVc9.55011$vY2.1335831@e3500-atl2.usenetserver.com> <3D759E65.2278934C@Azonic.co.nz> <Wucd9.4851$zv.733122@news20.bellglobal.com> <6w5f9.57758$2L.3039925@e3500-atl2.usenetserver.com>`

```
Donald Tees wrote:

<<That one dwells on this earth as a guest of the earth ... >>

to which Judson McClendon replied:

<<Except for the 'guest of the earth' part, I agree with the above ...
Historically, the idea that the earth is some sort of entity drrives from
the Gaia philosophy created by those who have practiced witchraft for
thousands of years.>>

I don't think I agree with this characterization of the Gaia hypothesis, or
its outright dismissal by this comment.

It seems to me that the traditional Judaeo-Christian writings that the
directive to subdue the earth and rule over all living creatures in it
(Genesis 1:28) implied a presumed quality of judgment and benevolence, and
an alignment with the will of the Creator, that no longer apply after
Genesis 3 (the Fall of Man).  Because of those latter events, I believe we
must avoid any hint of imposing *our own* will in the subduing of the earth
and in the ruling over all living things.  After the fall, the former will
by default lead to a "rape and pillage" attitude toward the earth, and the
latter to the wanton destruction of entire species of living things.  I
don't think either is what the author of this passage had in mind, and I do
think mankind's path, particularly in the last couple of centuries, is at
least in that direction.

And conversely I am not at all convinced that someone who treats the earth
as if it were a living thing (conscious or otherwise) is in violation of the
Judaeo-Christian writings on the subject.  I believe we are obligated by
those very points to bring a caution and care to that stewardship that has
been sadly lacking for a very long time.

There are two basic points to the Gaia philosophy:  (1) that the earth is a
living thing and (2) that all living creatures on it are its offspring.
While I personally do not agree with the second on theological/philosophical
grounds (just as there are dogmatic  perspectives of denominations within
Christianity with which I have serious disagreement), I think that treating
the earth with the respect one would give to a living thing is appropriate.

From my explorations of various Gaia websites (in particular
http://gaiawest.iinet.net.au/ ), it appears to me that the underlying tenets
are more similar to some Native American belief systems as well as Buddhism
and some other Eastern systems than to Wicca or other forms of witchcraft.
This website describes five unique features of the Earth that man is
systematically destroying, and while the uniqueness of these features and
the degree to which mankind has degraded them may be a matter of opinion, I
am not convinced they deserve outright dismissal.

Further, I am unconvinced that it is appropriate to dismiss the perspective
that (fallen) mankind has systematically exercized poor judgment in his
stewardship of the earth and runs the risk of planetary destruction simply
on the basis that the authors of "The Gaia Hypothesis" gained their
respective perspectives from witchcraft rather than (as historical reports
indicate) from their observations of Mars, Venus and Earth as atmospheric
chemist and microbiologist.   "Having some points in common with" is not the
same thing as "derives from".

Have you particular evidence that Drs. James Lovelock and Lynn Margulies
were practicing witchcraft when they published these hypotheses, or for that
matter for the thousands of years preceding the publication of their book?

What if "rape and pillage" the earth is not what the Creator meant by
"subdue"?   What if the behavior of a despotic dictator wasn't what the
Creator meant by "rule over"?   A great deal of harm has been done to this
planet in the last couple hundred years by mankind, and it is not at all
clear to me that Genesis 1:28 justifies that harm.  Certainly a perspective
out-of-hand that would encourage us to limit that harm deserves better
treatment than dismissal as witchcraft.  Do we as humans treat the earth,
and the living things upon it, as a gift from our Creator to be treasured
and to be changed and damaged as little as possible?  Is it antithetical to
Judaeo-Christian beliefs to *behave toward the earth* as if it were a living
being, even if we don't accept the premise that the earth *is* a living
being?   I for one think not.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu>

```
"Richard Plinston" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

No it isn't.  Neither God's laws or manmade laws make such a claim
or assumption.  Stealing bread because you are starving is *not* the
same as stealing a person's pension fund because you are greedy.
In the case of pioneers setteling in what is for all intents and purposes
wild country with maybe one person per 100 square miles or less is
*not* the same as invading an established country with military.  It
is not the same morally or practically.

> It may not be the same to _you_ but it is the same to those invaded.
> Eventually the 'encroachment' takes _all_, or all the useful,
> territory.

Right now in these United Stated, this very day, you can own a piece
or property, and if somebody moves onto your duly titled land, if you
do not make a complaint with a period of time, you *lose* the title to
that land and the settler gets to keep it.  Your viewpoint is at odds
with hundreds of years of legal precedent which says that moving
onto land that is not monitored and kept *is* legal.  And you can't get
angry and start killing settlers over it, or you will be tried for murder.
By the laws that I live under right this minute, I would not have the
right to do what the American Indians did when they became angry
about established settlements, some of which were hundreds of years
old.  I'm not defending any barbarisms committed by the settlers *or*
the Indians.  But the idea that the American Indians were only victims
with no fault of their own is pure revisionist B.S.
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote:
> 
> "Richard Plinston" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

This is confusing mitigation with justification.

Practically it is not, morally it is - IF the framework of ownership is 
there. "He who is faithful in little..."

> 
> > It may not be the same to _you_ but it is the same to those invaded.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> onto land that is not monitored and kept *is* legal.

That "precedent" is a construct of precisely those whom it favoured, so we 
cannot take its history as any support of its moral basis. In fact, if I 
recall correctly, one of the grievances of the American colonists against the 
Crown was that the system then did not after all permit such encroachment. So 
they changed the system; all that the change tells us, then, is what it was, 
not any justification for it. For that you must look more widely.

  And you can't get
> angry and start killing settlers over it, or you will be tried for murder.
> By the laws that I live under right this minute, I would not have the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> with no fault of their own is pure revisionist B.S.

But the question of whether THEY had justification remains open, under the 
whole set of facts and governing institutions. Sure, it is not established in 
their favour - but SETTLER laws against their acts are subject to the same 
circularity described above, and so do not in themselves show any fault in 
the retaliation. Again, for that you must look more widely than the law which 
helped suppress them. PML.
```

```
"Judson McClendon" <jud@sunvaley0.com> wrote 

> No it isn't.  Neither God's laws 

There is no such thing as 'God's laws'.  There are manmade laws in
statute books and manmade laws in various different old books.  I am
sure that you consider some of those old books to have special magical
powers while others are the work of the devils.  You are also probably
very selective in which 'laws' that you obey: do you grow a beard ? 
Do you eat bacon ?

One of the contemptable issues that I find is the attitude that "'God'
told me to do this" or "'God' gave me this" or even "'God' is on my
side so I can do what I like".  These seem to be the basis of your
'morality'.

Of course it is also the basis of the morality of 9/11.  'God' told
them to do that too.

> or manmade laws make such a claim or assumption.

Yes they do.  

> Stealing bread because you are starving is *not* the
> same as stealing a person's pension fund because you are greedy.

Yes it is, in _law_.  People have been hanged or transported for
stealing a loaf of bread, starving or not.

You may feel that morally people should not starve for want of a loaf
of bread, rightly so, but you are _wrong_ about the law.  Stealing
_is_ stealing.  I defy you to find some law that says it's OK to
shoplift as long as you eat it.

> In the case of pioneers setteling in what is for all intents and purposes
> wild country with maybe one person per 100 square miles or less is
> *not* the same as invading an established country with military.  It

cannot do anything wrong can you.

into 'wild country with only a few villages of palestinians' and
kicking them out is 'OK' because "'God' told them they could do it",
but blowing up restaurants and busses is completely wrong, immoral and
they should be punished (never mind that is what jews did in 1948).

> or property, and if somebody moves onto your duly titled land, if you
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…

Right now in the US (or at least in parts of it) if someone enters
your property for the purpose of stealing from you then you can shoot
them dead legally.

Your viewpoint is at odds with legal precedent that says you can
protect your property with force.

You seem to be very selective with the laws that you wish to keep for
yourself and those that you wish others to be able to use.

In fact the laws that you describe as making settlement legal was
passed by _settlers_ and was not part of the laws that Indians had or
agreed to, in fact they weren't even part of the law making process
having no vote even.

Your 'legal precendent' is just pieces of paper that can be waved to
'prove' you have the 'right'.

> But the idea that the American Indians were only victims
> with no fault of their own is pure revisionist B.S.

Only because it makes you (and many Americans) the culprits.  You
refuse to accept that you should feel any guilt because "'God' is on
_your_ side" and thus whatever is done is 'right'.

Did the Indians invade Europe ?
```

```
"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:
>
> See my other reply. What you say above is true. UNFORTUNATELY
> it is that attitude which will NOT win the US any friends - least of all
> from the rag-heads in the Middle East!

No attitude, simply statement of fact. :-)  I've come to believe there
is nothing we can do to make the people in the Middle East like us.
But we *can* make them respect us, or at least respect our military
power.  And we *will* eliminate those who are terrorizing us.  The
terrorists are making the same mistake that Japan made in 1941.
Americans don't like war, and often put up with a lot to avoid one.
But any time U.S. citizens and territory are attacked (unlike e.g. in
Korea and Viet Nam), The U.S. will do *whatever is necessary*
to eliminate the threat.  God forbid that terrorists sneak a nuke into
a U.S. city, for not only will we lose a city, but at least one country
will become a glow-in-the-dark parking lot.  I really, really hope
the terrorists understand that.

This is a grim subject, but that is why the American Indians were
virtually wiped out.  Yes, I know, there were horrible atrocities on
both sides, and I'm not justifying it.  I'm just saying that when the
Indians started attacking houses at night to kidnap and kill women
and children, they were certainly doomed.  There is an attitude
deep in the U.S. psyche that can be expressed something like "Hey,
you leave me alone, and I'll leave you alone.  I'll even go way out
of my way to help you when you need it.  But you mess with me or
my people, and I will clean your clock, however long it takes and
whatever the cost."  The U.S. doesn't 'get even', we eliminate the
threat.  Which is why Lend-Lease, Normandy, Hiroshima, Bosnia,
Afghanistan, and unfortunately, the American Indian.  The terms of
"unconditional surrender" was not a slip of FDR's tongue, he was
just stating what is intuitively obvious to most Americans.
```

```
"Hugh Candlin" <NoPersonalMailPlease@MyDot.Com> wrote:
>
> Judson McClendon <jud@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Respect, no.  Hate, yes.

Respect, yes.  I don't mean 'respect' in the sense of 'appreciate', but in
the sense 'wary of'.  Hate, already, what's to lose? ;-)

> > And we *will* eliminate those who are terrorizing us.
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> always believe they are in the right.

Yes. Yes. Yes. Yes. Yes.  I didn't say we would elininate anyone
who *might* attack us.  But you can be sure we *will* eliminate
those who do.  The U.S. has never lost a war where our security
was threatened.

> > The terrorists are making the same mistake that Japan made in 1941.
> > Americans don't like war, and often put up with a lot to avoid one.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> So, who are you going to bomb?

You can be sure that, if someone nukes the U.S., there will be a
*severe* price to pay.  Perhaps it escaped your notice, but when
that 747 was bombed over Scottland, it took a long time, but the
perpetrators *were* found, and Libya was behind it.  When the
World Trade center was bombed in 1993, *all* the terrorists
were tracked down, even though many of them were finally found
in other countries.  You can bet your last cent that if the U.S. were
nuked, the perpetrators *would* be found, if it took 50 years.  All
of these major terrorists have to countries that protect and fund
them, or they could not operate for long.  In a case like that, any
country, and I mean *any* country, who interfered with locating
those responsible would regret it, big time.  Such interference
would be taken by the American population as an act of war.  The
fury felt and expressed by the U.S. population over 9-11 stunned
even those of us who live here, including jaded members of the
press.  You cannot imagine the rage that would result from a city
being nuked.  The U.S. population would almost be willing to
destroy themselves if necessary in order to obliterate those
responsible.  What Japan did in 1941 was trivial when compared
to nuking a city, and the U.S. virtually destroyed their civilization.
Before the decision was made to use the atom bomb on Japan,
plans were being made to invade the island, with one million
anticipated U.S. casualties.  But, once the threat from the Japanese
and Germans was eliminated, we spent $billions helping Japan
and Europe to rebuild.  Not many (any?) countries who have been
attacked show such mercy as victors as does the U.S.

> > This is a grim subject, but that is why the American Indians were
> > virtually wiped out.  Yes, I know, there were horrible atrocities on
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> That's all the Indians wanted.  To be left alone.

I agree.  I wasn't trying to excuse the massacre, just trying to make a
point about why it happened, moral judgments aside.
```

```
number of injuries and deaths to U.S. forces was less than would have
happened by accident if they had simply stayed home.  As much as I

or in prison.  The U.S. government has stated clearly and repeatedly
from the very beginning that this effort will take years, and bin Laden
is only a tiny part of this.  There is no way the U.S. government is
going to announce that Osama bin Laden is dead until they have
absolutely incontrovertible proof.  That doesn't mean he is still alive.
```

```
"Liam Devlin" <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net> wrote:
>
> Do you remember that the Israelis unilaterally bombed a site years ago
> (80's?) at which Saddam was supposed to have been on the verge of
> nuclear weapons? Why wouldn't that be a useful tactic in 2002?

The nuclear reactor was easily found and targeted. The huge construction
process of such a thing, not even in secret, makes it a sitting duck.  But
bio and chemical warfare agents can be made in relatively small labs,
tucked away in any of millions of buildings. It takes hard, on the ground
intelligence to bomb those.

> Finally, where is it written that the heads of government of sovereign
> nations have to meet the approval of the US government? The arrogance is
> unbelievable, as is the hypocrisy, i.e., what about countries like China
> (the commies)? We never discussed declaring war on the USSR to topple
> "evil" leaders like Stalin.

Any government has it's own safety to consider, and when a foreign
leader is a threat, it is the business of government to deal with that
threat. This is the only legitimate reason countries maintain a military.
The U.S. is no different in that respect than any other government,
except that the U.S. has a bigger stick than anybody else, right now.

As far as China or the USSR are concerned, if a policeman catches a
drug pusher in the act, he might be able to simply grab and arrest him.
But if that same policeman becomes aware of a huge drug ring, that
approach won't work, even though the crime is the same. That also
goes for countries. This is only common sense, after all. Some things
are doable one way, other things require a different approach, even
if the reason for doing both is the same.
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote:
> "Alistair Maclean" <ld50macca@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
> 
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> with rush hour traffic.

The first jet hit the first tower at 8:45/8:46 and the 2nd at 9:03. 
There was rush hour traffic; but most people, by far, commuted to lower 
Manhattan underground (the NYC subway & the PATH). The towers were 
relatively empty (nowhere near completely). An attack at 11 AM or 2 PM 
would have killed far more people.


 From CNN for 09/12/2001:

http://www.cnn.com/2001/US/09/11/new.york.terror/

The assault on the towers began at 8:45 a.m. when a hijacked commercial 
airplane crashed into the north tower of the World Trade Center.

As smoke and flames consumed the building's upper levels and passersby 
1,000 feet below gathered to watch, a second hijacked airliner slammed 
into the south tower just 18 minutes later. It, too, burst into flames, 
shattering windows and raining debris.


From: http://www.thewtcmemorial.com/

In honor of all lost, there is a 6 month memorial that has been planned 
for the victims. At precisely 8:46 and 9:03 there will be two moments of 
silence. The nationally televised Battery Park tribute will also feature 
poetry, and words from Mayor Bloomberg, and two of the many gentlemen 
that led us though this difficult time, Rudy Guiliani, and Govenor 
George Pataki. We here at TheWTCMemorial encourage you to take part in 
the moments of silence, as well as watch the service.
```

```
In article <3D712377.D40671AA@shaw.ca>, James J. Gavan <jjgavan@shaw.ca>
writes
>
>Give us a little more background on this one. I checked my kiddy
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>guy who finished up building roads isn't he <G>).

Encyclopaedia Britannica:

Probably descendants of pre-Celtic aborigines, the Picts were first
noticed in AD 297, when a Roman writer spoke of the "Picts and Irish
[Scots] attacking" Hadrian's Wall. Their warfare with the Romans during
the occupation was almost continual. Then or soon after, they seem to
have developed two kingdoms north of the Firth of Forth, a southern and
a northern; but by the 7th century there was a united "Pict-land," which
already had been penetrated by Christianity. In 843, Kenneth I MacAlpin,
king of the Scots (centred in Argyll and Bute), became also king of the
Picts, uniting their two lands in a new kingdom of Alba, which evolved
into Scotland. 


>
>You seem to imply the Picts were not of Celtic stock - although we know the
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>Isle of Man fit in this group ?).
No, Vikings.

>
>See Bobbino - we are back to those Celts again <G>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Presidential eligibility.

_(reply depth: 22)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2002-08-30T15:26:29-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0208301426.342b398c@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<ak4c8s$1f0$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <217e491a.0208271936.1062236d@posting.google.com> <akif9c$s7o$1@panix1.panix.com> <3D6DCE40.3B4A00F3@Azonic.co.nz> <akk2t2$j91$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote 

> 
> I'm sorry, there seems to be some logic that escapes me... are you saying 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> discourse, please?

All elections have criteria for who is eligible to vote: age,
citizenship, residential status, etc.

Note that settlement didn't start in NZ until the 1840s (except the
Moari who started in approx 1000AD or so).

In the 1850s the criteria was that everyone who was over 21 and was a
land owner, or business owner or rent payer or some other minor
criteria could vote. This included Maoris and any other race but
excluded itinerants, visiting sailors, whalers and sealers.

Now it happened that women were not land owners or rent payers. That
is, it didn't necessarily exclude women because they were women, but
because they didn't meet the eligibility criteria.  This was corrected
by changing the eligability to one based on residency and specifically
including women.
```

```
"Liam Devlin" <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net> wrote:
> Judson McClendon wrote:
> > This is a grim subject, but that is why the American Indians were
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> they attacked or who got scalped.

Conquer, yes.  Virtual genocide, no.  Their culture was doomed from
the start, yes.  Any time an advanced culture encroaches on a primitive
culture, this is (for good or ill) the inevitable result.  Had the Indians
accepted it, they would have been absorbed into the new culture.  They
would have (unfortunately) probably been second class citizens for a
while, but not forever.  And they would not have been slaughtered as
they were.  No moral statement, just making a point.  Slavery, another
black mark on American history, could have been eliminated without
bloodshed.  It is a little known but documented fact that before the
civil war the southern states proposed federal legislation that would
have eliminated slavery over several years (and in 1860 the Virginia
legislature came within one vote of abolishing slavery in Virginia).
But the northern states killed it.  I'm not sure exactly why, but I think
it was because they wanted it stopped *immediately*.  However, this
would have destroyed the economy of the southern states, because so
much capital had been paid to northern slave traders to buy the slaves.
No doubt the northern states waited until the market on slaves was
down before promoting this action.  Actually, slavery in the Americas
was started by the colonizing European countries hundreds of years
before the U.S. revolution.  By the time the U.S. became a country,
slavery was already well entrenched in the culture and economy by
the British.
```

```
"Donald Tees" <donald_tees@sympatico.ca> wrote:
> "Judson McClendon" <jud@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
> >
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Primitive?  You mean that they had not progressed to mechanized warfare?

In this case, I mean primitive technologically.

> The north american culture was an peacefull stable agricultural culture for
> 12,000 years or so, and had developed mathmatics, medicine, and most of the
> current world food crops (all the bean and corn crops, about have of our
> grain) before the spanish came.  In many ways, it was far more advanced than
> the war culture that superceded it.


It is also true than many Indian women were treated as virtual slaves,
spending maybe 18 hours a day cooking, chewing leather so the braves
could wear soft moccasins, and generally supporting the men while
they ran around and fought among themselves. If you think the Indians
were all peaceful and kind and just, you have been reading too much
revisionist history. Some tribes were peaceful, but some were very
warlike. The Indian cultures were no better or worse (morally) than
similar cultures of the times. The mathematics you speak of was not
developed or used by the North American Indians, but by the Incas
and Aztecs of Central and South America. In addition to mathematics,
they also practiced human sacrifice on a vast scale, and some
anthropologists believe it contributed to the eventual destruction of
their civilizations. I live in an area that is rich with Indian cultural
influences. Many of our place names are still Indian names (e.g.
Chattanooga, Chickamauga, Tallapoosa, Tuscaloosa, even Alabama).
There is a pretty thorough curriculum of local history in the public
schools here. There are Indian burial grounds a short distance away.
I am pretty sure that my ancestry includes at least a few Indians along
the way, based on old family photos.
```

```
"Liam Devlin" <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net> wrote:
> Judson McClendon wrote:
> >
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Judson, you are truly an embarrassment in front of the rest of the world!

I hope they are enjoying the show.

But why? For making observations without moral assessments? I neither
contributed to, nor approve of, the virtual genocide of the Indians. But
there is nothing wrong with making observations about the mechanics
of how attrocities occur, even when you find them abhorent. A truly vast
number of books have been written about the attrocities the Nazis
committed. Understanding the mechanics of something helps to better
understand why it happened, and hopefully prevent its recurrance. I
would certainly hope that any condemnation I would make about the
Indian massacres would be entirely redundant. Or do you think there
are still people out there who need to be informed that it was a Bad
Thing?

> > Slavery, another
> > black mark on American history, could have been eliminated without
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> so-called investigations.

No, Congressional Record documents currently residing in the Library
of Congress.

> And slavery in the US was all the fault of the Brits, right.

No, but the Brits started it here, and in other places like Jamaica and
other Caribbean islands. One of the most popular Christian hymns
of all time, one that is familiar to many non Christians (e.g. the movie
"Star Trek II") is "Amazing Grace", written by a (previous) British
slave ship captain named John Newton, who composed it around
1760-1770, before the American Revolution. You can find more
details at: http://www.anointedlinks.com/amazing_grace.html.
British slavery was well documented, even in the popular British
fiction of the day. (e.g. Jane Austen's "Mansfield Park", published
in 1814). But it is clear that slavery was firmly embedded in the
Colonial economy well before the U.S. became a nation. That does
not excuse the Americans from continuing the practice after British
rule was overthrown.
```

```
"Donald Tees" <donald_tees@sympatico.ca> wrote:
>
> He can?  I thought he would need at least one other person to agree before
> he could kill anybody he wanted.  Perhaps the laws of the States should be
> ammended to at least the point where a madman cannot destroy the world.


For any extended action, the President needs approval from Congress,
because they can cut off the funding.  But for a limited action, all he has
to do is order it done.  In cases where the action would violate statutory
law, he can declare a 'True and Present Danger' to the security of the
U.S.  The U.S. Constitution places the military under the authority of the
President (he is the Commander in Chief of the armed forces)  The
current U.S. restriction on assassination is a Presidential Order (by
Gerald Ford, IIRC).  Any sitting President could undo that in 5 minutes.
When it gets right down to it, the President of the U.S. has the authority
to order a strike on just about anybody whenever he wants.  Of course,
he will pay the political costs eventually.  If a President tried to do
something seriously out of line, like nuking somebody without cause, it
is almost certain (IMO) that high level military and staff would refuse
the order and claim he was mentally imbalanced.
```

```
Richard wrote:

> "Hugh Candlin" <NoPersonalMailPlease@MyDot.Com> wrote
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> is just another visitor.

That's tellin' 'em Ricardo <G>. Yes the US is the most POWERFUL country
in the world economically and militarily - and following from that the US
president is powerful - *subject* to a consensus - as FDR had from the
Congress and Senate following December 7th (8th ?), 1941. Bill Clinton's
ideals of Health Care reform went nowhere without the blessing of the two
Houses; similarly, although supported by Clinton, the former SEC
chairman's recommendations were negated by both Republicans  and
Democrats voting against 'their' president..

By comparison the UK model - A nominal head of state, (the monarchy, not
a president), means that in the UK and Canada, and I'm sure the same
applies to Australia and New Zealand - the PRIME MINISTER has more
day-to-day power than the US president. The Prime Minister having got his
cabinet to agree (1) Can call a snap election anytime before his/her
mandate runs out, (2) At cabinet level decide whether or not to support
the US in the Hussein/Iraq affair, (3) Introduce Sociall/Health schemes
or Budget as they see fit, (4) Strengthen/lessen military logistics
etc..., the list goes on an on. When I say 'at the cabinet level', more
often than not this is an 'inner circle' of the more powerful within the
cabinet - not quite the same, but a bit like George W's 'Texas cowboys'.

Having ensured they have their party MPs on a leash, they can push
through any legislation they want. Neviille Chamberlain didn't ask
parliament in 1939, and Tony Blair didn't ask parliament in 199? - before
getting into WWII or the Gulf War. (Same with Maggie Thatcher and the
Falkland crisis). They inform parliament that this is what is happening,
just as FDR did back in 1941. It was emotion that carried through the
last event, regardless of rules/regulations.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
In article <akddqe$7ok$1@peabody.colorado.edu>, "Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote:
>
>On 25-Aug-2002, spambait@milmac.com (Doug Miller) wrote:
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>President or Speaker of the House or even do a Gerald Ford?

(Brief mea culpa: above quotation was typed from an obviously imperfect 
memory. Quotations below are copied directly from an authoritative source.)

Amendment 22, Section 1: "No person shall be elected to the office of the 
President more than twice, and no person who has held the office of President, 
or acted as President, for more than two years of a term to which some other 
person was elected President shall be elected to the office of the President 
more than once... " (The remainder of Section 1 is a disclaimer stating that 
this does not apply to whoever is President at the time the article was 
proposed or enacted.)

Amendment 12, Section 1, final sentence: "But no person constitutionally 
ineligible to the office of the President shall be eligible to that of 
Vice-President of the United States."

By the strict letter of the law, I suppose it might be possible for a former 
two-term President to be elected Speaker of the House, and succeed to the 
Presidency again if the President *and* the VP die simultaneously. I'd expect 
to see a Supreme Court challenge to that, however. And since the line of 
succession after the VP is established by Federal law, not by the 
Constitution, it would not surprise me to learn that such a situation is 
already provided for under the law.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT (sort-of) - Does it take math skills to do data processing?

- **From:** klatteross@aol.commmm (Ross Klatte)
- **Date:** 2002-08-27T01:23:17+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
>From: "Rick Smith" ricksmith@mfi.net 
>Date: 2002-08-26 20:24 Eastern Daylight Time
>

>For former President Clinton to become President again,
>she will have to run in her own name. <G>
>

Probably Nancy Reagan wielded a lot more power than the
woman from Arkansas.  

I am reminded of Lurleen Wallace.  When her husband George was
prevented by the state constitution from running for another term for
governor, she ran on a campaign pledge that she would do whatever 
her husband told her to do.  She won with 54%.  On those issues
which were attracting national news coverage, she apparently 
fulfilled her pledge.  On unimportant issues, such as care for
the mentally ill, she planned and was carrying out her own policy. 
Sooner or later, certainly, there would have been a clash.  Such 
an interesting situation was prevented by her death from
cancer at the age of 42.  

There is nothing in any constitution or statute that would prevent
this kind of shogun rule on a national basis.  If an outgoing 
two-term president were an admired ex-member of the television 
news media clique, then I think it could be very easily done. 
The constitution provides--in theory, at least--for temporary
replacement if the president is mentally or physically incapable
of carrying out his duties on the golf course.  But there is no
provision for dealing with a president who is fully capable of
doing his job but who voluntarily delegates it to someone else.  
The only thing that is actually required of a president is 
his signature. 



Ross
Roebuck, South Carolina
http://community.webshots.com/user/ross_klatte
```

```
In the book, "The Death of Common Sense" the author relates the following
story.

The city of San Francisco pays contractor $40,000 / year to remove dead
trees from city parks. One bureaucrat sent out a few letters and found that
there were companies that would PAY the city MORE than $40,000 / year to BUY
the dead trees, removal included!

Then it was pointed out to the bureaucrat that the city would have to spend
$100,000/yr in legal fees to defend the change in policy (citizen
complaints, environmentalists, losing bidders, etc.).
```

```
Sure I have a cite for this. I 'cited' the source of the story in my opening
sentence! Did it ever occur to you, in your wildest dreams, that I might NOT
have a cite? Did you think maybe I made it up? Or, alternatively, are you
eager to get this reference material for your own library? Makes no
difference; the book is available at your local library or bookstore:

THE DEATH OF COMMON SENSE
How Law Is Suffocating America
By Philip K. Howard
Random House, 1994, 202 pages
ISBN: 0679429948

I would be happy to dig out the page number on which this story is related
and provide the author's footnotes if necessary.

Howard had an even more fascinating story on the painting of the Brooklyn
Bridge in time for the bridge's centiennial celebration. It seems the
planning for its 100th birthday began four years before the event. But the
bridge looked really scruffy and needed a coat of paint! Based on past
experience, it would take five years from the time the bids went out until
the bridge actually got painted! (Most of the five years was spent in
appeals from losing bidders and the eventual lawsuits.)

The borough president said, "screw this," and hired a local contractor on
cost-plus basis to paint the damned bridge. The bridge was completely
painted - way in advance of the centiennial birthday party - before the
dozens of lawsuits could even get their first day in court.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT (sort-of) - Does it take math skills to do data processing?

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <jud@sunvaley0.com>
- **Date:** 2002-08-25T04:31:49-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<2r1a9.50474$%v4.1454120@e3500-atl2.usenetserver.com>`
- **References:** `<ak4c8s$1f0$1@nntp9.atl.mindspring.net> <1LL99.175378$SS.7195749@bin3.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com> <7WU99.168651$EJ4.4767931@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net> <m5X99.187722$SS.7669360@bin3.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com>`

```
Two more from N.Y.C. (apologies if anyone else has posted them on
this thread).  Sorry, I'm relating these from memory and don't recall
the references.

A portable toilet manufacturer, hearing that bathrooms were scarce
in downtown New York, approached the city with the intention of
donating hundreds of the things to be placed on street corners.  The
city said that by law each facility *must* have special accommodation
for the handicapped. The company installed a number of test facilities,
with the handicapped accommodation, and monitoring devices to count
how often the regular and handicap toilets were used.  Turned out, the
handicapped ones were virtually never used.  The city still insisted.
The company responded that the extra cost made the idea impractical,
and the project was scrapped, so nobody got extra toilets.

A company in New York owned an old office building, and rented it
cheaply to a religious order that did charity work.  The company gave
the building to the nuns.  The city said since the title was transferred,
the law required the building to have an elevator, because it was more
than three floors.  The nuns protested, saying they had been walking up
the stairs for years, their religious beliefs would prevent them using an
elevator even if it were installed, and certainly they could not afford to
install one.  The city condemned the building, and the community lost the charitable services the nuns had provided.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT (sort-of) - Does it take math skills to do data processing?

- **From:** Liam Devlin <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net>

```
Judson McClendon wrote:
> Two more from N.Y.C. (apologies if anyone else has posted them on
> this thread).  Sorry, I'm relating these from memory and don't recall
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> install one.  The city condemned the building, and the community lost the charitable services the nuns had provided.

I hate this senseless waste as much as anyone, but I think it's 
shortsighted to blame it on the bureaucrats who said they had to have an 
elevator. I browsed the NYC building code yesterday & today and, if I 
read it correctly, any building over 3 stories has to have an elevator. 
There's no provision in the code to exempt nuns from that requirement 
for a homeless shelter. There was probably a change in building class 
from the building's former use to being a homeless shelter. The 
bureaucrats are just following the rules. I believe this is a recent 
change to NYC's building code (the 3 story rule). However, I do find 
fault with the maze of laws & regulations, many of which are 
nonsensical; but they come from the lawmakers, not the bureaucrats.

I lived in the city for a while in a 6 story walkup and was told at the 
time that 6 stories was the limit for a walkup, i.e., 7 or more stories 
and the building had to have an elevator (there are lots of 6 story 
walkups around Manhattan). That would be for Building Class J, 
residential. The would be homeless shelter might have been classified 
some sort of medical facility, not residential, at any rate.
```

```
Well getting back to the topic (sort of)...whenever people
ask me this question my answer is... It does not take math
skills other than simple algebra to do business programming,
it takes logic skills. The computer does the math. If you
did well in geometry with all the proofs and theorems and
progression of logical steps involved you will probably
be a good programmer. It does not matter if you need a
calculator to do arithmetic, it matters that you know
the steps and requirements necessary to perform or program
that arithmetic.
```

```

"Ron" <NoSoy@swbell.net> wrote in message
news:ake7rb$p23$1@nntp-m01.news.aol.com...
> Well getting back to the topic (sort of)...whenever people
> ask me this question my answer is... It does not take math
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> that arithmetic.

I believe this to be true for many disciplines.  When I was
doing my physics undergrad, a pattern developed in the
first-year students.  (There are a lot of pre-engineers in that
group, as the curricula is nearly identical for the first two
years.)

First semester, everyone had a calculator.  Second semester,
about half didn't even bother to bring them anymore -- those
are the ones that would make it to year 3.  The other half,
were fervently looking to their calculators for answers that
their knowledge could not provide.
```
